Nine Workingmen Are Instantly Killed, and
of the Seriously Injured at Least Four

JOSEPH KING.
CHICAGO, Aug. 28.â Twelve steel
arches, each weighing thirty-
three tons, which were * to have
supported the superstructure of
the Coliseum building, in course
of erection on Wabash avenue, between
Fifteenth and Sixteenth streets, fell to
the ground late this afternoon. It is
known that nine lives were crushed
out. .The bodies of two of the men are
under the wreckage. Ten are in th"
hospital with injuries received in the
accident and of these four will surely
die. One may possibly recover and the
balance are for the greater part seri
ously injured.
Those injured not seriously are:
Cornelius Toomey, J. H. Warley, John
Hawthorne, John J. Johnson, David
Reardon, H. P. Zimmer, J. Flack, J.
Hanson and Andrew Morrison.
All of the twelve arches were standing,
the twelfth and last having been com-
pleted to-day. It was the intention of the
steel contractors, the Pittsburg Bridge
Company of Pittsburg, Pa., to turn over
its work to-night to the general contract-
ors. The iron' "traveler," or derrick,
which had been used in the erection of the
arches had been removed, and the agents
of the bridge company were accounting
their work as practically completed when
suddenly and without the slightest pre-
liminary warning the arch last put in
jilace suddenly fell over against the one
next to it. The weight was too much for
this and it gave way, crashing against the
third, and, one by one. the great steel
spans fell over to the south, precisely in
the- same manner as a number of cards
would fall. Nearly all the men who were
killed wen at work on top of the arches.
forty feet above the ground. Some of them
made futile attempts to slide down the
sides of the arches, but before they could
help themselves they were hurled to the
ground. Many of them uttered piercing
shrieks for aid as they fell. Most of them
were killed outright by the awful nature
of their injuries. The ski.is of the unfor
tunate men were crushed Into shapeless
masses, their limbs were cut off as if by a
monster knife and they were mangled be-
yond recognition. The Immense structure
began falling slowly, each arch being sus
tained by the crossbeams supporting the
rent of the structure. As the mass gained
impetus the bars of Iron, Inches thick.
snapped with a loud report, and the great
mass moved faster and faster in its de-
scent to the ground. With a crash that
wat heard blocks distant the structure
fell, leaving the walls practically unin
jured, and sending up great clouds of
dust and dirt.
An immense crowd gathered around the
place and despite the efforts of the police.
who were close at hand, they swarmed
over the mass of wreckage, making dcs-
perate but ill-directed efforts to drag out
the dead and to save the wounded. It
was only with t.ie greatest difficulty that '
the police were finally able t.i drive back
the crowd and give the firemen and un-
injured workmen a chance to rescue the
injured. ,
That more men were not killed and in-
jured was almost a miracle. Fully fifty
men were at work in the space covered by
the arches as they fell. A number of the
men fell Into small excavations, of which
there are many in the ground beneath the
steel works, and although some were bat
tered up by the fall these, were saved from
the great masses of steel that crashed
down over them. Several of them were i

i standing under the arches when they com
! menced to fall, and, running to the side
walls, by great good fortune happened to
. stand in the space left vacant by the
curve of the arches away from the side
walls. Had the arches fallen to either
side the loss of life must have been much
; heavier, but they fell through to the south
on a parallel to the side of the building
j and none of them save the two southern
arches fell at all outside of the space to
be occupied by the building. The direct
cause of the accident is not known. One
theory which received some support is
that a shifting of the sand beneath th*
foundation caused the collapse of th"
first arch, which brought all the ethers
to the ground. A story which cannot be
substantiated is that a number of work-
men were using a pulley on the north arch
; while they were taking down some scaf-
folding that had been erected around it.
j They caused the arch to sway and finally
it swayed too far and fell out.
"I cannot say what caused the acci
i dent." said Superintendent Johnson. "I
could hardly believe my eyes when I saw
' the results of our weeks of work de-
stroyed in a minute."
The work of rebuilding will commence
! at once and it is expected that to-day's
accident will delay the opening of the
building not over one month. The financial
loss will not fall upon the Coliseum Com-
I pany, which is erecting the structure. Tne
only loser will be the Pittsburg Bridge
Company. The company's loss is esti
. mated at 125,000. The Coliseum will lose
indirectly financially through inability to
arrange for attractions .during the com
, ing winter season. The unfinished bund-
ing occupied the ground on the east side
of Wabash avenue, between Fourteenth
and Sixteenth streets, on which Libby
Prison formerly stood. The structure was
304 feet long by 172 feet wide and when
completed would have been 85 feet high.
i It was designed for the accommodation
of large conventions, expositions, circuses,
football games and other affairs of great
public attraction.
A singular fatality has attended all
buildings in Chicago called "coliseum."
The first to be erected stood on Sixty
, third street, and like the present Coliseum
it collapsed while being built in the fall of
1594. The side walls of the building had
been erected and eleven steel arches had
been put in place. It was desirable that
it should be speedily finished and th-
work was pushed night and day. It was
i the custom of the contractor to allow
his men time for lunch between 11:30 nnd
midnight. During this half hour, while
all the men were absent, all the arches
fell to the ground, injuring nobody, but
causing Â» pr<nÂ»erty loss of about $30,000.
The bui.JuiS was rebuilt <*;,.. the last
Democratic National Convention was held
within Its walls. The second Coliseum
was totally destroyed by fire on the night
of December 24, 1897, and was never re
' built. The accident to-day brought the
1 third Coliseum down, which will te re-
built at once. '
NEW COPPER COMPANIES
ARE ORGANIZED
Standard Oil Company and the Roths-
childs Believed to Be Interested
in the Venture.
NEW YORK, Aug. 28.â The Journal and
Advertiser to-morrow will say:
Articles of incorporation of three new
i copper companies, capitalized at several
, millions, and behind which are said, to be
the Standard Oil Company and the Roths-
childs' interests, were yesterday filed in
the office of the Clerk of Hudson County,
N. J.
One ls the Augrand Conner Company,
1 with a capital stock of $1,000,000. The in
corporators are â  Charles B. Burrage,
Charles N. King and Edward Story. The
same capitalists, with the addition of W.
11. Broughton and Nathan F. Leopold, are
the incorporators ofl the Oxide Copper
Company, with a capital of $5,000,000.
The third concern is the Island Copper
Company, which starts with $100,000 cap-
ital, which, however, is to be increased to
$10,000,000. The Island Company has the
same incorporators as the others, with
George H. Sisson added.
DRUNKEN QUARREL
ENDS IN MURDER
LOS ANGELES. Aug. 28.â Dead Mans
Canyon is about seven miles north df
Newhall and has a few inhabitants.
Among those were Jean Delbaty. his wife
j and daughter, and his friend, Gus Chaud
foze. a miner. The men yesterday pro
cured a demijohn of red wine. They par
took freely of the beverage and by night
fall were In a quarrelsome mood.
They continued their quarrel until be
tween 7 and 8 o'clock. Suddenly, it Is
said, Chaudfoze seized a shotgun and
fired at Delbaty. The load of buckshot
took effect in Delbaty's right groin and
lower bowels, tearing away the intestines,
death ensuing instantly. Immediately
' after the murder was committed Chaud
foze walked to the ranch of Louis Gauilie
I and informed him of the, murder. He
I made no. attempt to avoid arrest.

SAN FRANCISCO, TUESDAY, AUGUST 29, 1899.
BRAKEMAN
AVERTS RAIL
COLLISION
Monterey Flyer Ditched to
Prevent Heavy Loss
of Life.
ENGINEERS ESCAPE
t
Man Who Saved the Trains Struck
Man Who Saved the Trains Struck
by Plying Debris, but Not
Seriously Injured.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, Aug. 2S.â Quick action
on the part of Brakernan Beachler
of the narrow-guage train averted
a horrible railway accident at
Santa Clara to-day. As it is, two
monster engines are piled one upon the
other in a ditch beside the track and
the baggage-car is wrecked.
The northbound Monterey Flyer.
nearly an hour behind time, which
leaves here at 9:12 for San Francisco,
was derailed at the crossing of the
broad and narrow gauge tracks at
Santa Clara this morning to prevent it
from crashing into the narrow-gauge
j passenger train bound for this city.
j Rules are strict in regard to stops be
; ing made at a crossing. The Flyer was
] drawn by a double-header and was at
j tempting to make up time. The nar
; row-gauge passenger had just left the
Santa Clara depot. Brakernan Beach
: ler went ahead to turn a switch and had
entered the semaphore house and made
! the switch. The signal was given to
I the narrow-gauge to come ahead.
Beachler looked up the track and saw
! the Flyer coming. He signaled to
Engineer James Stanley of the narrow
: gauge to stop and the engineer re-
versed his train and started back.
There was only one thing to do to
I prevent a collision and Beachler acted
; quickly. He opened the switch and de-
railed the Monterey train. Both en-
gines left the track, followed by the
| combination express and mail car and
the forward truck of the baggage-car.
Engine 1377 was ahead, in charge of
Engineer Fred Simpson and Fireman
Consell, and engine 1372 which followed
! was manned by Engineer Fred Stewart
and Fireman Judd. As soon as the en-
gineers saw the signal to stop they re-
versed their engines, but it was too late
to stop the heavy train. They set the
air-brakes and jumped, escaping with
only a* few scratches and a general
shaking up. The passengers were
nearly thrown from their seats by the
sudden jar, but none were injured.
Engine 1377 went in the ditch and
[ rolled over on its back and the one be-
I hind climbed right up on top of it.
There they lay locked in a death-like
embrace.
Brakernan Beachler as he left the
signal house was struck by flying tim-
bers and injured about the head. He
was picked up unconscious and brought
to this city. His injuries are not seri
ous. His first inquiry was "Did
I have my flag?" showing that he had
thought to save life by throwing the
switch. Trains were delayed for sev
eral hours, but the wreckers now have
the track clear.
Four years ago a bad wreck occurred
between passeneer trains at the same
crossing. At that time many persons
were seriously injured. Engineers
Stewart and Stanley who were on to-
day's trains were in that wreck, the
latter being nearly killed. Another co-
incidence is the fact that the numbers
of both wrecked engines began with
unlucky 13.
Complete files of The Call contain- <
ing accounts of the welcome home to
'. our heroes can be obtained at Call
Business Office. Send a copy to your
j Eastern friends.
QUIET IN SAMOA.
All the Officials Are Working Har
moniously.
APIA, Samoan Islands, Aug. 23, via
Auckland, Aug. 28.â A1l is quiet in Samoa.
The officials are working harmoniously.
Business is prosperous and a large
amount of copra Is being made.
The only warships here are the German
protected cruiser Cormoran and the Brit
ish armed sloop Torch. The United States
cruiser Abarenda has arrived with the
material for the coaling station and jetty
at Pago-Pago.

SPARKILL. N. V., Aug. 28.â
fire that broke out at 1 o'clock
this morning destroyed nine of
the ten buildings of the St. Agnes'
Convent and Orphanage, entailing
a loss of $150,000 and causing the death
of four persons. Besides the dead and
seriously injured, twenty-five inmates
were injured by falls, jumps and burns,
none seriously.
That more lives were not lost was
due to the heroism of the Sisters and
some of the older Inmates. When the
fire broke out the 400 inmates of the in-
stitution were asleep. Most of them
were got out in almost- perfect order,
but a score v^- c risked .hei- lives to
save others were finally forced to either
jump from the upper stories or make
desperate dashes through stairways
and corridors filled with flames and
smoke.
The fire was discovered in the lava-
tory and soon spread to the adjoining
buildings, attacking first a dormitory
in which 200 of the older boys slept.
Sister Reginald, who had trained the
lads in the fire drill, rushed down the
line of white beds and arousing the
sleepers, clapped her hands, the simple
fire signal followed in the orphanage.
The boys turned out with a rush and
gained the lawn in their night clothes.
By that time the second dormitory,
where the smaller boys slept, was
ablaze. The older lads made a dash for.
the building and were soon carrying
the smaller ones out. The buildings
were all finished with Georgia pine and
the wood burned furiously.
The convent and chapel made a great
stack of flame, and between them and
the second dormitory* the girls' dormi-
tory blazed and crackled as the fire
leaped up its wooden walls.
A dozen Sisters were cut off in the
upper stories of the convent building.
They were forced to the windows, and
while some climbed out to the sills,
others knelt at the casements in pray-
er. Engineer Otto placed a long ladder
on the outer wall and rescued half a
dozen. Six were forced to jump for
their lives and all but. Sister
Bertrand escaped without serious
Injury. The escape of Sister Agnes
seemed almost a miracle. She was cut
off on the fourth floor and elected to
jump from her window rather than
plunge into the furnace that had once
been a stairway. She climbed, out on
the sill and then, seizing the shutter.
swung out clear of the building. Then
she loosened her hold. She struck the
ground squarely upon her feet and
then fell over on her side. Before any
one could reach her she got up and
walked away unhurt.
The fire reached the girls' dormitory
last, but once it took hold of the build-
ing it burned fiercely. Most of the girls
were on the upper floors and it was
with the greatest difficulty that they
were got out. Shortly after the last
of the*' rescued left the second dormi-
tory there was a counting of heads and
the discovery was made that two baby
boys were missing. John Cody, a 15
- boy, made straight for the
door, paying no heed to the warnings
of danger. A moment later Cody came
dashing out of the building, under each
arm carrying one of the missing boys.
Firemen came from Piedmont and
Nyack, but the best they could do was
to save the hospital, a large two-story
building.
The Sisters and children were con-
veyed by teams to the Blauvelt Con
vent. As far as known but oue person
actually perished in the fire itself.
That was "Jane," an old pensioner,
aged 70 years, who was asleep in the
servants' quarter. Helen Brown and
Emma Mackin died from convulsions
that resulted from fright. Kate Mc-
Carthy was a servant in the convent
and had organic heart trouble. She
was badly frightened, collapsed in the
grounds and died later in the day.
It is believed Therese Murphy and
Mary Brown both escaped from the
fire, but that the former became de
mented and is leading .the other, girl
through the woods. . - â¢ J .-â¢
There were 326 children in the or-
phanage, sixty of -whom were girls and
their ages ranged from 2 to 16 years.
TRANSVAAL AGAIN HURLS
DEFIANCE AT ENGLAND
Boer Government Notifies Chamberlain That It
Will Make No Further Concessions.
Burghers Are Exchanging Their Martinis for Mausers and Dili-
gently Continue Their Preparations for the Clash That
They Believe Is Certain to Come,
PIETERMARITZBURG, THE CAPITAL OF NATAL.
This city is the headquarters of the English army in the colony. It contains an arsenal, magazines and large bat
racks for all branches of the service. From a sketch made by J. Harrington several years ago.
CAPE TOWN, Aug. Replying to the. latest proposition of the British Secretary of State for the Colonies.
Mr. Chamberlain, the Government of the Transvaal has notified him that it adheres to its latest offer and will
not make any further concessions.
According to reports from Johannesburg, the burghers are everywhere exchanging Martinis for Mausers.
A heavy load of ammunition, Including 300 Mausers, arrived at Johannesburg on Friday from Utrecht, the
Netherlands.
The Cape House of Assembly is against discussing the question of transit of arms intended for Orange Free
State and presumably the Transvaal. Evidently a state of extreme tension exists as every speech made to-day
was received with almost absolute silence in the chamber.
William P. Schreiner, the Premier/personally appealed" to his followers to refrain from answering the criticism
of the opposition lest they should prove words, of ill feeling.
The entire opposition arose to support the motion for adjournment made by Right Hon. John Gordon Sprigg,
the former Premier. Sir John, who repeated the arguments he had. used in the previous debate, asked the Premier
what action the Cabinet proposed taking in the matter. He said that the recent speech of the British Secretary of
State for the Colonies. Sir Joseph Chamberlain, at Birmingham, England, had forced all to the conclusion that South
Africa was on; the brink of war unless the Transvaal met the -British demands. In his -opinion the Cape Govern
ment ought to', follow the example of Portugal at Delagoa Bay and refuse to permit the transit of arms as long as
the crisis lasted. - â¢ â¢*.".- v\ .- - -.- â 
After Sir John Gordon Sprigg had finished a pause occurred, the -house waiting for the Premier's . reply.
Finally Mr. â¢ Schreiner rose and declared, that under the customs convention the Orange Free State was justified
in importing arms through Cape Colony and he did not intend to tear up that. convention, knowing, as he did, that
there would be no war. ,*â  â¢.*â '' - â¢".'â¢. "_â  -,* â¢ . â 
"In the event of war," he said, "I would not lack .the .true Imperial spirit."
Mr. Schreiner then read a letter from President .Steyn of the Orange Free- State, denying any intention on the
part of that Government to take up arms. in any aggressive or offensive manner against the ' British Government
or the Cape Colony. It was the final policy of the Orange. Free State, President Steyn continued, never to take up
arms except when attacked or in support of its-treaty-obligations. The letter created a profound impression as
clearly showing the purpose of the* importation of- firearms. Several members dwelt upon this phase, and Mr.
Schreiner promises in the case of future consignments to consult the Governor; of Cape Colony. Sir Alfred Milner,
which he admitted he had not previously done. He also asserted that the Cape Colony would maintain neutrality
in the event of war. . .- â "'. ' â¢! ' *â¢
LONDON, Aug. 28.â The St. James Gazette to-day says' that' .it 'learns that an inspection iby General Joubert,
commander in chief of the Transvaal force, disclosed the fact that nine-tenths of the reserve Shells and cartridges
in the Pretoria forts are inefficient and that an immediate order to renew the supply was placed in Europe.
The Cape Town correspondent of the Daily Chronicle says: British troops will be immediately concentrated
at Laings Neck and Mafaking, and it is believed that the British programme will be to surround the Transvaal and
then to negotiate a. new. convention. ... ..'.,:
Although not definitely known, it is assumed here in London that Mr. Chamberlain 'had the reply of the
Transvaal Government ,before he delivered the Birmingham speech. It is also believed that on the receipt of Presi
dent Kruger's offer of a five years* franchise and other concessions, Mr. Chamberlain wired that they were unac
ceptable â¢ and suggested modification. If this be so, and if the reply to that suggestionâ that the Transvaal Govern
ment adheres to its latest offer and will make no further concessionsâ be President Kruger's final answer the posi
tion is ominous. ...
The importance of President Steyn's letter to Mr. Schreiner lies in the fact that an offensive treaty exists be
tween'the Transvaal and the Orange Free State. .

NEW YORK, Aug. 28.â A cablegram
received to-day by the representative
of the Dominican revolutionists in this
city from Santiago de Cuba stated that
the Government had surrendered to the
forces lof General Jiminez. While the
authenticity of the report is questioned.
advices from . the Herald's correspond-
ent. at San Domingo, which have been
delayed in transmission, state that on
Friday the revolutionists were only a
few hours' distant from the capital and
that the Government was tottering.
Senor Grullon, the recipient of the
cablegram, said:
- "There is no question in my mind as
to the truth of this cablegram. Mr.
Osmun Le Marche is an agent of the
Dominican revolutionists and would
not for the world think of sending any
such message if it were not true. There
has been to my mind no fighting. The
whole people have arisen and over-
turned the Government. It was a
peaceful revolution. The people have
no faith in the present Government of-
ficials, who were the servants of the
late President Hereaux, and combine
all his bad qualities without having his
ability.
â _ Senor Grullon said: "So far as my
plan has been considered, it is safe to
say that for the present some provi
sional government ' will be established
until the general election is held. I
know certainly that General Jiminez
will not assume any offlce until he is
'chosen at an election by the people.
He wants to be the popular choice. He
does not want jto ride into offlce by
force or by any sudden coup. He vill
wait for the election and he will be tri
umphantly elected. The people want
him. GeneraL Jiminez is not a profes
sional revolutionist. He is known in
New York and. has been engaged m
professional enterprises since 1875. He
is a man of high ideate and wants to
give to' the San Domingo Government
free and civilized rule that may enable
the 'people of his native land to pros-
per." : _^________

Special Cable to The Call and the New Tork

HONGKONG, Aug. 28.â censor
ship in the Philippines continues un
changed. It is political instead of
military.
The only friendly natives I found on
my southern tour were those at Moros,
who so far are unwilling that we should
hold any territory except the single
walled town of Jolo. Even the non
combatants hate us. In Manila the na
tive feeling against us is growing
stronger every day. Taxes are higher
in Manila than under Spanish rule and
the inhabitants bitterly complain. Liv
ing expenses have doubled. The native
police have been discharged and Ameri
cans : appointed, whose administration
is tyrannical and inefficient because
they are ignorant of the language and
not familiar with .the perpetrators of
crime.
Murders and robberies, the old in
habitants say, are more numerous than
ever before. Taxes and customs duties
are being used to support the army in
stead of being applied to municipal ne
cessities. This really prolongs the war
by keeping the natives irritated after2

THE NEW ALABAMA AND HER PREDECESSORS.
l Alabama is not a new name in the American naval register. In 1818 the keel was laid fora llne-of-battle ship ln the
l Portsmouth Navy Yard. After a time work was suspended, and only resumed in a desultory manner as the years went
l by. She was listened Alabama and was a sister ship to the Vermont, which had been completed at the Boston yard the
l year the Alabama was commenced. When the war of the Rebellion broke out in .8.1 the name Alabama was changed to
). New Hampshire, and the work of the builders was rushed, so that the craft was completed and went Into commission early
lZ in 1*64. She never saw any active service, as when she was ready for sea the day of the sailing vessel had long passed by.
â  She is at present in use by the New York Naval Reserve, and entered upon the register as "unserviceable." . The most
l famous of the vessels bearing that name was the Confederate cruiser Alabama, which was sunk off Cherbourg, France, m
_. July. 1Â»64, by the U. S. S. Kearsarge. in command of Captain Winslow. after an action lasting sixty-five minutes. The
> new Kearsarge is ring completion at the Newport News yard, and at no distant day the Alabama and the Kearsarge
> may be seen in one fleet, both flying the stars and stripes and ready to engage a common foe.

tract requires a sneed of sixteen knots.
The contract price of hull and ma
chinery is $2,650,000. This is exclusive
of armament and other new features
of equipment to be added by the Gov
ernment at Washington.
The contractors are confident the new
vessel will meet every requirement as
to speed, *â¢ worthiness, etc., specified
in the contract.
According to experts the armor of the
Alabama is the most complete ever put
upon a battleship.' It is of Harveyized
nickel steel. The side armor is 16%
Inches thick at the top of the belt, and
tapers to >'*i Inches at the bottom. In
the turrets the porthole plates are 1.
inches thick, and other plates 15 inches.
The protective deck rises from the
lower Inside edge of the water-line belt
upward, and inside to a level with the
top of the belt, and forms an armored
flat, the slope being 4 inches aft. 3
Inches forward and on the flat 2%
inches thick.
Another novelty on the Alabama Is
the placing of her two smokestacks
abreast of each other instead of along
the axis of the vessel. In some respects
the Alabama resembles the lowa,
though the new battleship is eight feet
longer and several inches narrower
than .Her predecessor. \

- - Zealandia. -
WASHINGTON, Aug. 28.-A dispatch
from General Otis, dated the 26th inst.,
has been received at the War Department
giving the details of the troops on the
transports Valencia and Zealandia. It is
as follows:
The Valencia sailed August 23 with 16
officers and 324 enlisted men of the Mon
tana Infantry; 10 officers and 86 dis
charged men, two hospital corps and Aye
civilians. The Zealandia sailed August 23
with 24 officers and 339 enlisted men of the
Montana Infantry*: eight officers and 179
discharged men. eight men of the hos
pital corps. 20 enlisted men sick, and nine
civilians. The Montana Infantry left in
Manila 5 officers and 62 enlisted" men re
enlisted; 9 sick; 2 officers and 90 enlisted
men discharged. '."

Party of Blue Jackets Landed From

SHANGHAI, Aug. 28.â Advices. received

APACHES ON
THE WARPATH
IN ARIZONA
Outbreak of Hostilities With
the Zunis Momentarily
Expected.
BITTER TRIBAL FEUD
â Â« â
Braves Swear to Wreak Terrible
Vengeance -for- the Murder of
Four of Their Tribe. ;'
Special Dispatch to The Call.
JEROME, Ariz., Aug. 28.â Jerome was;
thrown into a fever of excitement to-day *
by a band of Apaches in war paint pass
ing through the town. Leaders of the
tribe .topped and purchased* a large sup
ply, of ammunition and. hastily left in the
wake of their fast-disappearing com- â 
rades. Additional interest was lent to the '
occurrence by the presence r of- only
"bucks" in the band, -
A reporter of the Associated Press se- j
cured a horse anc overtook tne. Indians a
few miles from Jerome, and after consid
erable persuasion was permitted to ac
company them on the journey of twenty
miles over the roughest imaginable coun
try. When the vicinity of Red Rock was
approached the pace of the horses wa.-
quickened' and an impatience was noticed
11. the Indians riot noticeable beforel. â  In
a canyon with perpendicular walls rising
to a height of hundreds ~i feet were the
lemains of a campiire and near by a wig- I
warn. In front of the wigwam was the
body of a squaw and papoose, while a *
few rods in front was a buck. All three j
hail been horribly .mutilated and scalped.
The hands and feet were rut off.
The Indians formed a circle and on the I
dismembered bodies swore to wreak yen- j
beance. The name Zuni occurred many'
times. The bodies were burled near the
scene of the crime and the band took the
trail to the north. 1ie.,.! Justin, one of
th.- most intelligent of the band, and who
speaks excellent English, was delegated
to accompany the reporter to the bounds
of civilization, and of him something of
the affair was learned.
The. Zunis are a New Mexico tribe, while
the Apaches are of Arizona. A bitter en
mity has existed for years between the
tribes, but a truce has put an end to hos- i
tilities for some time.
The three murdered Indians had left
their reservation near Camp Verde and
were surprised by a band of Zunis. The
Apaches took their trail In the direction i
r.f New Mexico, and old settlers fear that j
a furious outbreak will follow and end In
the extermination of one of the tribes.
There are no soldiers within reach, and
settlers are not disposed to interfere in
the outbreak. i
SEARCHING FOR
THE DERELICT
>
Unsuccessful Voyage of
the Willapa.
â â¦ â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
VICTORIA, B. 1., Aug. 28.â The mys
tery of the derelict barkentine which is
reported to have played tag with the reefs
off CtayotJUOt three weeks ago Is still un
solved. Indeed It grows more mysterious.
The steamer Willapa, which went as far
as the north end of Vancouver Island to
search for her, ha- returned, and neither
saw nor heard anything further of the
wandering barkentine. But although she
saw nothing of the vessel, the Willapa
saw campnres along the beachâ fires
which looked as though they were the
temporary homes of a shipwrecked crew.
They were over forty miles from any hab
itation and on a seldom visited part of the
coast. To see if there was any sign of
lift-, the steamer bore well inshore and
the officers scanned the beach with their
glasses, but without avail. If any ship
wrecked mariners had been there they
were not then visible, and It was too
rough for a landing to be made to In
vestigate further.
Another story which is coupled with the
mystery is that told by Captain A. L.
Hammen of the schooner Lily L, which
has returned from Unalaska. He tells of
sighting a derelictâ it maybe the same one
seen off Clayoquot â on August 16, in lati
tude 49 degrees and 58 minutes north,
longitude 136 degrees and 'd minutes west.
about 350- miles from the north end of
Vancouver Island. She was a three-mast
ed barkentine, square rigged. The lower
part of the mainmast was- standing, on
which was a gaff!- The hull was badly
broken and a great deal of the planking
gone, showing the vessel's ribs. She
seemed to be loaded with lumber. Another
search wil be made for the wandering
vessel. *

AGAIN VISITS NICE.
Admiral Dewey Accepts the vita
tion of the XJ. S. Vice Consul.
NICE, Aug. Admiral Dewey, ac
companied by Lieutenants Brumby and
Caldwell, arrived here this afternoon. Ac
cepting the United States Vice Consul's
invitation, the admiral drove to Point
Anfree, stopping at the drotto. Thence
he ascended the Fallcon, from which there
is a superb view. The party continued on
to Cimiez, skirting the heights over, the
town and returning to the city. In spite
of the warm weather and the fact that
this was his second visit, Admiral Dewey
expressed admiration of all he saw. The
American officers returned to Vlllefranche
after dinner. - ;-.-

FRENCH OFFICERS DECLARED OUTLAWS.
Captain Voulet and Lieutenant Chanoine. French army officers, are ac
cused of having killed Lieutenant Colonel Klobb and .Lieutenant Meunter.
The former two officers were in command of a mission in the Soudan, and
reports came to France of terrible cruelties being perpetrated upon the na
tives. \ Lieutenant Colonel Klobb and Lieutenant Meunier were sent to inves
tigate. As they approached the mission they were ordered to halt by Cap
tain Voulet. . The officers. made known their identity, and proceeded toward
the mission. " Immediately Captain Voulet and. Lieutenant Chanoine gave the
order to fire to their squad M men. with the result that Lieutenant Colonel
Klobb and Lieutenant Meunier were killed.
McLEAN IS NOW
ON THE SCENE
â â¢
Ohio Democratic Clans
at Zanesville.
â â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
ZANESVILLE. Ohio. Aug. 28.-The
Democratic State Convention was Inaug
urated to-day by the arrival of John R.
McLean this afternoon and Colonel
James R. Kilbourne to-night and all the
other candidates for Governor and other
places on the State ticket. While the at
tendance is unusually large there are not
many delegates present. They will come
to-morrow with the visiting clubs. The
preliminary meetings do not occur until
4 p. m. to-morrow and the convention does
not convene till Wednesday. There are
probably no changes to-day in the fig
ures. The arrival of McLean has made
his friends more enthusiastic and the ar
rival of Kilbourne has made some of his
friends more desperate. The other can
didates for Governor are not doing much
except shaking hands, with the excep
tion of Sherwood, who seems to be in
line for second place. The McLean men
are so confident of not less than seventy
six more than is necessary on the first
ballot that they are figuring on their
running mates. They prefer Colonel
James Kilbourne for Lieutenant Gov
ernor, but so far neither he nor his
friends will concede fhe nomination to
McLean or accept the second place. If
the Kilbourne men hold out it is likely
that the ticket will be McLean and Sher
wood.
The McLean leaders held a general con
ference late this afternoon and gave re
assurances afterward of a majority of
seventy-eight over all, together with
claims of seventeen sure and probably
eighteen of the twenty-one Congressional
districts. This would Insure them the
control of all the committees to-morrow
night and absolute control of the conven
tion.
Judge William T. Mooney will be tem
porary chairman of the convention, but
he is not wanted by the McLean men for
permanent chairman. Congressman J. D.
Norton will be the permanent presiding
officer.
The candidacy of McLean has brought
many prominent men here that have not
attended conventions for many years.
Some of them are from other States.
Nearly all the "old guard" that was in
McLean's organization in 1897. when he
sought to succeed Sherman as Senator,
are here and all of the workers he mar
shaled two years ago against Hanna are
here. Kilbourne has not only the old
friends of Thurman. but quite an army
of new men of his own following. All
of the candidates seem to have "root
ers" available.
The indications to-day are that there
will be opposition to the adoption of the
proposed constitution that provides a
more definite plan of party organization
and a uniform system for selecting dele
gates to all Democratic conventions.
FAMILY FEUD.
LOUISVILLE. Aug. 28.â A special to the
Times from London, Ky., says:
â¢ News has been received here confirming
a report that a pitched battle took place
Saturday between James Hayes and his
three sons on one side and Senator Will
iam Clarke and Sherman Leadford on the
other, on the streets of McKee, Jackson
County. Clarke received three pistol
wounds and his condition is critical.
Young Hayes was shot In the breast and
will die. Bitter feeling of long standing
between the two families, which are the
most wealthy in Jackson County, caused
the shooting.

RULINGS AS TO
RAILWAY RATES
Decision of Commerce
Commission.
â â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, Aug. 2S.â The Inter-
state Commerce Commission to-day an-
nounced its decision in a case involving
the relative rates on export and domestic
traffic in grain and grain products.
The commission holds that in the ab
sence of some justifying reason it would
not be right for American railroads to
permanently transact business for for- '
eigners at a less rate than that for which
they render corresponding service to j
American citizens. As to rates on ex- 1
port and domestic shipments of grain the !
commission decides:
Market conditions sometimes, in case of
wheat but seldom in case of corn, may- ;
justify an export rate through the port '
of New York somewhat lower than the
domestic rate, and Philadelphia, Balti-
more. Norfolk and Newport News usually I
take rates which arc certainly differen
tials below the ' New York rate on both
I domestic and export traffic. During the
J period of closed lake navigation the ex-
port and domestic grain rates to New
York and the other ports mentioned
j should ordinarily be the same. Rates to
j other ports. including Boston and ports
! on the Atlantic north of Boston, and Gal- |
veston. New Orleans and other gulf ports,
i may perhaps be properly made lower on
export than on domestic traffic, to en
; aid.- them to compete for the export busi-
ness. Such an adjustment of the rates
| would be to the advantage of the carrier,
I and just alike to the American consumer
; and the American producer. But as the
problem is primarily one for the carriers
I rather than this commission, no order is
| made in relation to this branch of the
i case. But the export grain rates should
I not be less to the seaboard from any point
j than from an intermediate point of the
j same line.
As to the rates on export flour as com
: pared with export wheat, the commission
i holds that public policy and good railway
1 policy alike seem to require the same rate
| on export wheat and export flour, but in
I view of the conditions shown in the inves
tigation a somewhat higher rate on .ex
; port flour than on export wheat is not in
j violation of that statute. The published
I difference in rates is too wide, and the
rate on flour for export should not exceed
that upon export wheat by more than 2
cents per 100 pounds. The relation of rates
on domestic shipments of flour ahd wheat >
: is not Involved In this decision, as the ex-
port and domestic freights are handled
under different conditions.
It further appeared that carriers en- i
gaged In the transportation of export flour
from Minneapolis at a rate which was 1^
; cents -less than the domestic rate to the .
; port of export refuse to make any corre
sponding concession to intermediate mil
: lers. The commission says that this is |
I unjust and unlawful discrimination
: against such intermediate traffic, and that
whatever line participates in such lower ,
; export rate on flour from Minneapolis j
, must .make a corresponding rate upon
* similar traffic from intermediate points.
The commission also decides that rates '
on export traffic must be published and
filed in accordance with the provisions of
section 6 of the interstate commerce act. i
It is further held that so-called through !
! export rates, made by adding the ocean I
rate, whatever It may be, to the Inland
I rail rate, whatever it may lie. are not â 
; analogous .to rates made by joint arrange- I
ments between railroad carriers subject
I to the statute in the sense that the total
j rate must be published and filed, and it
i is enough If the railway carrier publishes
! and maintains its own rate- to the sea- j
i board. But if there is, in fact, such a
| joint arrangement that the rate is a joint ,
| rate under the sixth section of the act to i
regulate commerce, then ,- the entire !
I through rate should be published, and not i
! the Inland division, which in that. case
i might vary while the entire rate remains *
the same. - . . . s

Special Dispatch to The Call.
RENNES, Aug. 28.â balance
of the evidence to-day, for a
change, was in favor of Dreyfus.
Five witnesses were for him and
two against him.
The most interesting testimony was
that of Chief Handwriting Expert
Charavay, who had come to declare that
he had changed entirely his opinion,
which in 1894 was against, and is now
in favor of Dreyfus, who he to-day af
firmed .was not the author of the bor
dereau. His candid confession of error
was received with murmurs of satis
faction in court, which became discreet
applause in spite of Colonel Jouaust's
patent disapproval, when he solemnly
added:
"I declare here, on my soul and con
science, that the bordereau was writ
ten by Esterhazy."
The most important incident, how
ever, was Colonel Jouaust's acquies
cence to Major Carrlere's request that
a rogatory commission be Instructed to
take Colonel dv Paty de Clam's depo
sition. The initiative came purely from
the Government Commissary, Maitres
Labori and Demange having no faith
ln such a measure, because it allows Dv
Paty de Clam to escape cross-examina
tion, which is the only thing worth
having under the present circum
stances. Dv Paty de Clam being a wit
ness for the prosecution, Major Car
riere will simply prepare a list of ques
itons which an examining magistrate
will put to Dv Paty de Clam at his resi- |
dence and nobody supposes that the
witness will be very much embarrassed
by the interrogatories.
To-day the central figure in the
courtyard, which is the meeting place j
for all the leading personages of the j
trial during the suspension of the ses- j
sions, was Captain Freystmetter, who i
was the subject" of many flattering re
marks on his manly and soldier-like
bearing. Indeed, the interest in him j
was so great that some persons waited j
all night long outside the door of the;
court in order to obtain standing room |
in the rear part of the courtroom, in \
the hope of seeing him at to-day's sit- |
ting, an impression having gone abroad
that he might be recalled. His modest
and frank manner inspired admiration
in all except the generals and the other
military witnesses for the prosecution. i
who seemed afraid of him and scowled ;
in his direction from the other side of !
the courtyard. Captain Freystmetter I
will not remain here until the end of
the trial, but will leave Rennes in a few- j
days. \.r
M. de Freyclnet, formerly Minister of j
War. arrived this afternoon and will |
testify to-morrow.

Bertillon's Testimony Punctured by

RENNES. Aug. 28.â When the fourth
week of the second trial by court-martial
of Captain Alfred Dreyfus of the artil
lery, charged with treason, opened in
the Lycee building this morning General
Mercier, who was Minister of War when
Dreyfus was first tried. In 1894, was pres
ent ib usual In the front row of the wit
ness' seats, thus definitely setting at rest
the reports that he had fled to the Isl
and of Jersey and as to his arrest.
The first witness to-day was M. Paray-
Javal. the draughtsman, whose evidence
was Interrupted on Saturday by the ad
journment of the court. M. Paray-Javal
was called by the defense to refute the
system by which M. Bertillon, chief of
the anthropometric department of the
Paris Prefecture of Police, last week at
tempted to prove that the prisoner wrote
the famous bordereau in a disguised
hand and by means of the key-word
"interest," and that it could not have
been written by Esterhazy. although the
flocument was in a handwriting resemb
ling that of Esterhazy. With the aid of
a blackboard M. Paray-Javal demonstrat
ed the fallacy of M. Bertillon's calcula
tions and criticized the latter's unfairness
ln not subjecting Esterhazy's handwrit
ing to the same tests as the prisoner's
writing. At the same time tne draughts
man declared that even if M. Bertillon
had done so the results would not have
pfoved anything. â '*-
In brief, the morning session may be
said to have been a field day for the
handwriting experts, who devoted them
selves to demolishing one another's evi
dence. The public followed the deposi
tions listlessly, but the judges and coun
sel listened with the greatest attention.
General Mercier did not seem to be very
cheerful to-day. He hardly said a word
to General Gonse during the whole morn
ing, but during the brief suspension of
the court's sitting he and Generals Gonse
and Reset walked ip and down the court
yard of thp Lycee engaged In conversa
tion Genera] de Roisdeffre-was not pres
ent in court to-day. He has been absent
irom the past few sessions, and lt is
rumors] here that he is desirous of leav
ing tne sinking ship 0f the general staff.

Every morning Captain Dreyfus leaves the military prison for the Lycee, where the trial is held, at a quarter past
6. the sitting of the court beginning fifteen minutes later. He is attended by military warders, and on his way to the

M. Charavay, the archivist and reporter

"In 1894," said M. Charavay, "I, with
two colleagues, though acting under sepa-
rate instructions, was commissioned to
examine the bordereau and a number of
documents for comparison, unsigned and
In different handwriting. 1 examined first :
the latter documents, and by the process
of elimination fixed upon one resembling
the bordereau. I was then furnished with '
specimens of the handwriting in question,
but was not told the name of the writer.
1 asked if the document could be regarded ,
as genuine, and was told the place whence
it emanated, which could Dot be mentioned !
by me and which could leave no doubt in
regard to Its value. I make this remark ;
; because I think it explains my opinion, |
for I could not consider that a document
of this nature which was not marked by
a certain dissimilitude of handwriting. I :
therefore attributed to the dissimulation
the differences I was careful to note in
, my report. I must inform the court that
in view of the fact that handwriting which
was not produced in 1594, and which is cvi- |
dently akin to the handwriting of the bor
; dereau, .as the handwriting of Dreyfus,
has since been submitted to me, I cannot i
maintain with the same degree of certain-
ty the 'conclusions of my former report,
and I can only make one statement, name-
ly, that those two handwritings resemble
the bordereau. ;:'.;â¢*
"I should, however, point out one of the
typical dissimilarities upon which 1 laid
stress in the writing of the bordereau and
the documents submitted for comparison,
j namely, that the double 's' is not found
I between the bordereau and the new hand-
I writing. In other words, the double V in
the bordereau is found in Esterhazy's
j writing. . ,T. ..

DUKE OF ORLEANS
LEAGUED WITH GUERIN
Such Is the Belief Said to Be Held by
the Police Authorities of
Paris.
NEW YORK. Aug. 28. -A Sun cable from
Paris says: The Courrler dv Soir says it
has learned of the importance which the
Government attaches to th? siege of the
house in the Rue de Chabrol in which the
anti-Semite leader, Guerin, and a number
of his friends have intrenched themselves
against the authorities. If true, it ex
plains the reason why the place was not
attacked days ago by the military.
The paper declares that the Government
believes that one of the pretenders to the
French throne, presumably the Duke of
Orleans, is in the house with Guerin, and
It wishes to capture him alive, in view of
the proof that his presence would furnish
of the monarchist conspiracy. If an as
sault should be made on the place It might
happen that the pretender would be killed.
and this the Government wishes to avoid.

WOODLAND. Aug. 28.â A special to the

WIFE SLAYS
HUSBAND IN
JEALOUS RAGE
Thomas J. Cook, a Peatlands
Farmer, Shot While
He Slept.
HAD A BAD RECORD
â¦
The Girl In the Case Flatly
Denies the Charges Made By
Mrs. Cook.
â  â â¦ â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA ANA, Aug. 28.â Thomas J.
Cook, a Peatlands farmer, recently ac
quitted of the murder of Jasper Grlggs
by of the same place, was shot" and
killed by his wife Katie this morning.
Jealousy was the cause and trouble
had been brewing for some time. Cook
was one of the best-known men In the
county and had the reputation of being
a gun fighter and a reckless man. He
had resided in this county eighteen
years, and in that time had engaged in
a deadly fight with his brother, each
emptying his gun with intent to kill.
without harm resulting. For some
time he and his wife had been on ill
terms because of alleged Infidelity on
his part. 'â  : .-
Cook was found this morning by
alarmed neighbors lying on a mattress
in his wife's room, with a bullet hole in
the corner of his right eye, stone dead.
The wife says Cook went out last night
[ and did not come home until 3 o'clock
jin the morning. When he returned she
I upbraided > him for infidelity and was
| told to do the next best thing if sh-
I didn't like it. Cook threatened to do her
j bodily harm if she exposed him. They
; quarreled, and at length Cook fell
j asleep and his wife took a gun from
j beneath his pillow and shot him. The
| shot alarmed the hired man, who
aroused the neighbors.
The girl in the case is Mabel Moody,
apparently about IS years old, and the
daughter of a rancher living some two
I or three miles southeast of the Cook
j home. She flatly contradicts Mrs. Cook
] and also says that Mr. and Mrs. Cook
I quarreled almost incessantly, and that
; Cook accused his wife of being too inti
j mate with the hired man? /.:/"â¢",'.
Much interest is manifested over the
shocking tragedy as Cook, although a
. turbulent, reckless man, had many
-. friends who stood by him through the
', trial in which he was acquitted of the
| murder of Jasper Griggsbv, and who
: still stoutly aver his innocence of any
; thing more serious than justifiable
j homicide in that matter.
Mrs. Cook also has many friends in
! the neighborhood, where she is well
known a.-*- the daughter of J. H. Pope, a
prominent farmer of that section. She
Is Cook' second wife and is but 28 years
old and had been married to Cook for
nine years. Cook has one brother in
Tulare County and has no other known
relatives in this country. Mrs. Cook
was brought to this city is now at the
County Jail. The witnesses to be called
jat the inquest to-morrow , are James
: Barton, Mabel ; Moody. SG. Vf. . Moore
and W. M. mann. The preliminary
j trial is set for August 30. Prominent
I counsel has been retained to represent
: the prisoner. The defense will be emo
\ tional insanity and the feeling to-night
j is strongly in the woman's favor.
CHINAS EMPEROR FEARS
HE MAY BE POISONED
Declares That Treacherous Ministers
Who Hate Him May Attempt
to Destroy Him.
TACOMA, Aug. Oriental advices per
! steamer Tacoma state: The Emperor of
| China, Hwang Hsu, is now provided with
j three meals a day. at each of which he
! is only given two kinds of vegetables and
j a small bowl of rice. It Is even suspected
! that an attempt is being made to starve
| and make his Majesty even weaker than
he has been. To a confidential eunuch
his Majesty recently said:
"My restoration to power is only a
question of time, hence I am only "too
anxious to maintain my health and bide
my time. I am only afraid that the one
or two traitorous Ministers who hate me
may by their machinations succeed ln
destroying me, and I have no means of
counteracting their treachery."
A report has just come of a serious riot
In Chinese Thibet at a place called Paao
An, some 130 miles over the border from
! Kansu. The Christian and Missionary
! Alliance has had a station there for about
, two years or so. and this has been at
tacked and looted by the Thibetans. The
missionaries, the Rev. George T. Shields
i and wife; with Mrs. McÃeth, barely es
caped with their lives to Lancheo. where
the China inland mission has workers.
This party of missionaries left Central
j China in the fall of last year and had
* consequently not been at Paao An for
more than a few months. The natives
had shown themselves . rather trouble
some, and to make matters worse the
Chinese officials, on being appealed to re
: fused to help or protect at all, and no
: doubt the riot was largely due to their
action.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
HULL, England. Aug. Walter Well-
man, the leader of tUe Wellman Polar ex- |
pedition, who returned to Tromsoe, Nor- ;
way. August 17, after having successfully j
completed operations in Franz Josef Land, '
has arrived here. IJe walks with the aid
j of crutches, his right leg. which was se-
! riously Injured by a fall into a snow-coy-
| ered crevasse while Mr. Wellman was
, leading his party, still being useless. The i
i explorer was accompanied by the Ameri- i
! can members of the expedition, who are
i well. In an Interview Mr. Wellman said:
"The object of the expedition* was two-
i foldâ to. complete the exploration of Franz
Josef Land, of which the north and
northeast parts were practically unknown,
and to reach a high latitude, or even the
pole itself. The hrst object was success-
fully accomplished. The second would
have been achieved, at least to a greater
extent than by previous explorers, but
for the accident to myself."
Mr. Wellman recounted the starting of
the expedition from Tromsoe June 26,
1898, how it reached Elmwood, the head-
quarters of the Jackson expedition, July
?S, and hoisted there the stars and stripes
i underneath the Union Jack, which had'
I been flying over the deserted settlement
] for nearly two years. The provisions left
; tor Professor Andree, the missing aero-
i naut, were found "by Mr. Wellman un-
] touched.
After leaving letters about his expe
dition and taking a collapsible house with
him, Mr. Wellman proceeded due east
i for Cape Tegethdff and arrived there July
30, TS9S. There the party landed all the
; stores and the steamer returned to Nor-
, way.
"We established our headquarters in the
: little house brought from Jackson's head-
quarters," continued Mr. Wellman, "over
i which, flew the Stars and Stripes.
"Desiring to push further north with
I the greatest speed. I dispatched Baldwin
! August 6, with the Norwegians, sledges,
I dogs and boats, myself and others intend-
! ing to follow in a few days.
"Shortly after Baldwin's departure, I
; discovered that, owing to a sudden break
[ up of the ice. 1 was unable to proceed, but
Â«I sent two Norwegians to inform Bald-
win to build his outpost, leave two men
; in charge of it, and return to my head-
quarters."
Here Lieutenant Evelyn B. Baldwin of
j the bureau told of The doings of his ex-
[ pedition. He said:
- "September 21 my party reached Cape
I Heller, in latitude Sl, where we decided to
; make an outpost.' From the very outset
. we had a constant fight with the high
: seas, ice and wind, and there was a con-
; tinual fog. The Ice underneath us parted
* with terrific crashes, leaving us adrift on
â  small floes. Here we built a station and
j called it Fort McKinley. We stocked it
i with blubber and meat, and leaving there
i two volunteers, Ventzen and Biorvlg, I
i started to return October 22 to Mr. Well-
j man's headquarters, arriving there Octo-
I ber 30 in safety, in spite of great hard-
! ships, cold, darkness and ice.
Â« "The . party spent the winter comfort-
j ably in H^rmsworth House, which was
completely nuried in snow. They killed
I many bears.
"Mr. Wellman, . February 18. 1899, with
j Norwegians with sledges and dogs,- start-
ed north." â â¢-â .
Mr. Wellman, continuing, said: "Feb
ruary 26, 1890, I arrived at Fort McKin-
ley. Bjorvig, rifle in hand, stood at the
j mouth of the snow tunnel. He wrung my
haryi and with tears in his eyes said:
'Poor Ventzen is dead.' Â»;â¢.
" "Of course, you have burled him,' I
said.
" 'No,' Bjorvig replied, lie lies there.*
pointing to the 'hut. 'I have kept my
, promise.' .x_X2
"The two men had made a compact that
In case of death the survivor should keep
! the body until help came. In the little
j hut the quick and the dead had slept side
:by side through two months of Arctic
: darkness.
"Bjorvig said he had managed to keep
: up his spirits by reciting Ibsen's poetry.
"The next day we gathered stones and
: under these we buried our dead comrade.
! A few fitting words were spoken as we
1 ; stood around ln 70 degrees of frost. After
' ; a delay of ten days the party. Including
Bjorvig, pressed north In sledges and by-
march until we reached S2 degrees, east
!of Rudolph Island. The prospects then
were most reassuring. We had three
I months of the best season before us and
| were confident of reaching 87 degrees.
1 Though we had suffered from cold we
; I were all In fine form.
"Then a seemingly trivial accident turned
- the advance into a precipitate retreat.
; While struggling with the sleds in rough
: : ice my right leg was bruised and sprained
by my falling Into a hidden crevasse. For
' ! two days I went on, and unless other cir-
! cumstances bad occurred 1 should
have pressed onward so far that I would
never have been able to return alive. At
: | midnight. March 22. we were awakened
, by the crushing of the Ice under our feet.

"I still believe it is possible to reach the
pole by Franz Josef Land, but I cannot
say if I shall make another effort."
After Mr. Wellman's return to Harms-
worth House April 9th Baldwin and four
Norwegians went out to Wilczek Land.
, charting the unexplored east coast and
discovering a new ice-covered island ex-
tending to 64 degrees east, almost as large
as Wilczek Land. They named it Graham
Bell Land, after the president of the
American Geographical Society. 3aldwln
returned to the base April 30th.
A. Harlan of the United States Coast
Survey also took a separate trip before
the reunited exposition sailed July 27th
on the steamer Capella.
A fairly complete survey of the archi
pelago has been prepared by Mr. Wellman
and his party. They all show traces of
hardship. Their fares are pale and drawn
and Wellman is much thinner than form
erly. His right leg is drawn up a foot
from the ground and it is not known
whether he will ever again use it. He will
consult a specialist in London Imme
diately. Otherwise he Is In good health
and spirits.
On board the El Dorado, the Norwegian
mail boat which brought the party to
Hull. Mr. Wellman was the center of at-
traction and on arriving at Hull, the pas-
sengers gave him a cordial send off.
The party proceeded immediately to
London, where they arrived this after-
noon.
PRESIDENT JORDAN IS
BACK AT PALO ALTO
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Aug. 28.â
President Jordan returned to the univer
sity to-day after a four weeks' camping
trip in the Kings River Canyon and among
the mountains of the high Sierras. He
was accompanied by Mrs. Jordan and by
the other members of the partyâ
fessor and Mrs. E. P. Cubberly, Pro-
fessors George M. Richardson, V. L. Kel
logg, G. H. Marx, A. V. Babine and Mr.
Swain and Mr. Jarnick. All the members
of the party are in the best of health.
The party camped most of the time in
the upper valley of the Kings River amid
wonderfully beautiful forests, making oc
casional excursions to the regions above
the snow line. They climbed a number or
high mountains, the highest and most re
markable in the view from it being Mount
Stanford, climbed first and named by Pro
fessor B. C. Brown of Stanford In 1896.
President Jordan climbed three peaks
that had not previously been ascended
and the other members of the party
climbed still others. He also prepared a
special map of the Mount Brewer Basin
and its surroundings, while Professor
Kellogg prepared several additions to the
maps of the other parts of the region.
Professor Brown discovered and mapped
a large basin containing many lakes which
region had hitherto been left blank on the
maps. -WrtÃ '-'-..-
Another Stanford party was under the
direction of E. B. Copeland '9,", a member
of the faculty of the Chico Normal School.
but just selected to the chair of botany in
the University of West Virginia. Copeland.
accompanied by Maxwell Adams of
Stanford and Mr. Henderson, a Uni
versity of California graduate, all
of Chico. climbed the very high
i point. hitherto inaccessible. at the
junction of the King and Kern divide
with the main Sierras. To this he official
ly gave the name Junction Peak, deposit
ing the pass cylinder of the Sierra Club
on its summit, with appropriate records.
From the University of California Pro
fessor J. N. T.e Conte. with a company.
was in the same canyon and made "many
ascents besides numerous observations
and records tending to perfect the maps
previously published by the Sierra Club.

BROKE THE SUNDAY LAW
Valuable Fishing Nets Confiscated
Near the Sacramento River.
Deputy Game and Fish Commissioners
Davis and Cross have confiscated nearly
$800 worth of salmon nets, which have
been turned over to the â -.: .- at Valle
jo. The seizures were made because of
the violation of the law which prohibits
the taking of salmon between sundown
on Saturday evening and sundown on
Sunday evening. The deputies suspected
that nets were in several of the sloughs
up the river and on Saturday night they
went in the State launch Quintal into
Montezuma Slough, where they waited
until a proper time to make a run through
those waters. After proceeding a short
distance they found a new net worth
about $350. of aliout 2400 feet in length.
This was taken on board.
A run was then made to what is known
as "the cut-off," where two more nets,
one new, if about 400 horns in length,
and the other of about 300 fathoms. Con
sidering the value of the property and
the fact that the fall run of salmon is
now on it is possible that the unknown
owners of the new nets may make appli
cation for their fishing rigs and take the
chances of being arrested, it' they do
they will surely fall into the clutch of
the law. for warrants have been sworn
out or them. If they can secure the
minimum line of $200 they will be getting
off lucky, but the chances are against
this, for the offense is likely to be tried
in the Superior Court, where less mercy
would be shown to the violators of the
law. .
TENNIS CHAMPIONS HERE.
Arrive Prom the East and Will Go to
Del Monte.
The tennis champions of America arrived
Inst night on the Central overland and are
at present stopping at the Palace Hotel.
Tlie party consists of George Wright of
the firm of Wright & Ditson; Beals C.
Wright, the scholastic champion: Hol
conibe Ward of Soul I Orange, N. J*. Mal
colm D. Whitman of Boston, the present
champion of the United States, and
Dwight P. Davis of St. Louis.
As jet no definite plans have been ar
ranged, but it Is very likely that the party
will leave for Del Monte this afternoon,
where they will remain until the doubles
championship tournament on September 9.
Exhibition games will he played among
the Easterners and match games be
tween the Eastern champions and the
Western cracks. The VVhitneys and
Hardys have been chosen to meet the vis
it.el.-.

THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, AUGUST 29, 1899.
LOCAL PUGILISTS TO
FIGHT IN STOCKTON
? FRANK RAPHAEL, Who Is Matched to Box Ruf Turner.
â¦ â¦ â¦ âÂ»*-â â¦-â¦-*â¦-Â»-â¦ â¦*>â¦â¦â¦ â Â» â¦ â¦ Â» <i â¦-*$- *-&-*-& -â¦-â¬
TWO boxing matches were made yesterday that will attract a great deal of
interest In the sporting world. Frank Raphael, the local lightweight, who
recently returned from Klondike with new fistic laurels and plenty deal of
interest In the sporting world. Frank Raphael, the local lightweight, who
recently returned from Klondike with new fist ie laurels and plenty of gold
dust, will meet Ruf Turner, the colored wonder, before the Stockton Ath
letic Club on September 19, and "Cocker" Tweedie, the Australian whirl-
wind, will box Sammy Maxwell before the same club on September 23. As
both goes will be pulled off during fair week it is expected that they will attract
large bouses.
Raphael and Tweedie left last night for the scene of their contests In company
with Tim McGrath, the well-known trainer. The men will Immediately go into
training tinder the watchful eye of McGrath. Raphael Is so confident of victory
that he has backed himself to the extent of $1000. Turner has easily found back
ing for the same amount. Besides fighting for a percentage of the gate receipts,
a generous side bet will tempt the lightweights to renewed exertions, and a splen-
did bout will result.
Raphael Is even now in splendid trim. The hard work he has done In the
northern regions and the excellent care he has taken of himself has left him in
good, enough condition to enter the ring at a moment's notice. Refore he left this
city for Alaska he had defeated Sammy Maxwell and other well-known men. and
while in Dawson be cleaned up large sums by getting draws with Pat Rooney, a
heavy-weight, Dick Agnew of New York and'knocking out other men. Last spring
he fought Agnc^w again and secured the decision in the eleventh round, the referee
stopping the bout and declaring him the winner. The sum he realized in that
fight was one of the largest ever received by a lightweight, it being $3200â loser
taking $1600. If successful in his bout with Turner, Raphael intends going East
with McGrath and taking on some of the leaders In his class. He claims he Is feel
ing stronger than ever before in his life and is confident he will make a good
showing. â¢ .
McGrath will join Joe Kennedy, and will assist "Spider" Kelly In training the
local heavy-weight for his bout with Maher.
Tweedie and Maxwell will fight for a percentage of the gate receipts. "Cocker"
swears that he will train hard for the contest, as he knows he has a hard man In
Maxwell. He Is in need of funds and promises to give a good account of him
self. Maxwell has improved wonderfully since his bout with Raphael some years
ago. and will undoubtedly make it warm for Tweedie. It is probable that many of
the local sports will run up to Stockton and witness both goes, as the men are good
ringsters. They will all fight at 135 pounds.

Local Turf Items.
The racing will begin at 1:30 o'clock at
Oakland to-day.
On Wednesday there will be a stock pa
rade in the forenoon.
After winning the second heat of the
pace, El Diablo pulled up so lame that the
Judges allowed him to be drawn.
It if said that Tommy Keating offered
$5000 for Clipper. last winter. The offer
was refused.
In "Monk" Coburn, the firm of Atkin &
Lottridge are ape to have the making of
another good rider. He put up a very
creditable ride astride Twinkle Twink.
Neerata, a starter in the 2:18 trot this af
ternoon, is pronounced* a corker. She
stepped two heats In a winning race at
Santa Rosa in 2:13%, 2:12%.
The well-known turfman, Dr. Rowell.
has been appointed official veterinary oi
the California Jockey Club. He has exam
ined all of the horses now quartered at
Oakland track and reports them free
from disease. As the Eastern horses ar
rive they will also be compelled to pass
musterâ a very wise precaution on the
part of the Oakland association.
Jockey Jimmy Bozeman, reinstated
some two months ago by the Turf Con
gress, is on his way back here from Chi
cago. He will be seen in the saddle at
Sacramento.
Duffy, the Montana rider, thinks he has
a kick coming. About two years ago he
was suspended by Captain Rees at Ingle
side for an indifferent ride. Last summer
Duffy received what he supposed to be a
conditional reinstatement, permitting him
to ride in Montana. It now transpires
that the reinstatement was an uncon
ditional one, and now "he Is wondering
why he did not become aware of the fact
last winter.
A carload of horses arrived at Oakland
track from St. Louis on Sunday night,
several different owners being repre
sented. 8. N. Holman brought over old
Benamela and Indro. Tom Boyle after an
unsuccessful trip came back with Ace,
Moringa and an Artillery colt. He later
shipped to Sacramento. Billy Randall's
string consisted of Ostler Joe. Montanus,
Miss Rowena and Balista. Jockey Devln,
under contract with Boyle, rode at Oak
land yesterday.

Grouse From Lassen County.
D. E. Allison, a commission merchant at
Front and Washington .streets, was ar
rested yesterday by Deputy Game and
Fish Commissioner 'C, A. Vogelsang for
violating the game laws, which prohibit
having grouse in possession before the
opening of the season, which begins on
September 1. Allison had a lot of grouse
in his store last Saturday that had been
shipped to him from Lassen County. He
was very angry at Mr. Vogelsang, whom
he took for the Commissioner, and
threatened to make things so hot for him
that he would have to resign.

Striped Bass Seized.
C. A. Vogelsang, Deputy Game and Fish
Commissioner, made a seizure of small
striped bass at the ferry yesterday. The
law prohibits the taking or having in pos
session of bass under three pounds in
weight, and although there are plenty
of larger fish of this kind in the. rivers
and bay still the fishermen never throw
the smaller ones back into the water,
but prefer taking the chances of being ar
rested and losing the fish as well. Tho
fish secured yesterday were sent to the
Hebrew Orphan Asylum.

George A, Knight Is Named for Attorney of
the San Francisco Board of Health,
Directors of Deaf and Dumb
and Blind Institute.
THE Governor has appointed
George A. Knight of this city to
the office of Attorney of the
Board of Health, vice Garret Mc-
Enerney. The office is easy in the
respect that the occupant has nothing
to do but receive the salary which is
Bent to him every month. Originally
the attorney was obliged to personally
apply for his salary, but as this pro
ceeding Involved a little time and
physical exertion, provision was made
to forward the compensation to the at
torney at stated periods. It is said to
be eminently proper that Mr. Knight
should hold the office, as the place was
created for him by statute. He was the
original holder of the position. When
his term expired Dennis Spencer of
Xapa was appointed his successor.
When Mr. Spencer died the vacancy
in the office was filled by the appoint
ing of Garrett McEnerney. It Is highly
creditable to Mr. McEnerney that he
gave the salary attached to the office to
the widow* of Mr. Spencer. In the of
fice of Dennis Spencer Mr. McEnerney
received his first lessons In the law, and
a sense of friendship for the family of
his teacher prompted him to bestow the
official emolument where it would have
been bestowed had Mr. Spencer lived
to serve out the term.
Fred V. Meyers of Stockton was yes
terday appointed by the Governor to
the office of Commissioner or" the Bu
reau of Labor Statistics, to succeed E.
F. Fitzgerald. The appointment was a
surprise to every one outside of the
Burns coterie in the yellow building.
There were many candidates for the
position, among them Wallace Pond of
Hand, A. J. Read of Oakland and
C. W. Kyle, chief clerk of the Assem
bly. Information came to The Call not
long since that Mr. Read was offered
.the place on certain conditions. He
"was sound * to ascertain if he would
agree to appoint "deputies rom a list
to be iced in his hands. The prop
GEORGE A. KNIGHT, Attorney of the Board of Health.
ositlon was not acceptable to him, I
her.ee his name was not inscribed on j
the commission.
At the Palace and Grand hotels, j
â where the politicians and office seekers j
meet to exchange views, the appoint- j
ment of Mevers is spoken of as further |
proof that th- Southern Pacific Rail- I
road is the dominant power in State j
affairs.
The people of Stockton were surpris-
ed, as the following dispatch indicates:
STOCKTON. Cal.. Aug. 2S.â The an-
STOCKTON. Cal., Aug. 28.â The an-
nouncement that Gage had appointed
Fred V Meyers of this city State Labor
Commissioner came as an all around sur-
prise, if nything the Governor does is
capable of exciting surprise. Leading;
Republicans set out to Inquire who the ap
pointee was. for. although raised in this
city. he is entirely unknown in political
circles So for as is known no State.
county or city leader was consulted. As
most of the Republicans do not know who
Meyers Is the news excited no comment
pro or con as to his fitness, but there was
much comment to the effect that the Gov
ernor in hi.s abnormal anxiety to appear
Independent had evidently picked put a
dard horse. The appointee is chairman
of the grievance committee of the South-
crn Pacific Chapter of the Brotherhood of
Locomotive Engineers. In this capacity
he is very close to the railroad power and
his mysterious influence most likely came
from the big yellow building where so
many appointments are decided upon.
Personally Meyers is spoken of as a nrst
class man. He is the son of the one-time
Assemblyman, Samuel Meyers of San .
Joaquin County, and was born and raised
in this county. He entered the service ot
the Southern Pacific when a mere boy. i
He became a railroad engineer and served
In that capacity for twelve years, running
on the Southern Pacific line in and out .
of Stockton. He took a prominent part ;
in the doings of the Brotherhood of Rail-
road Engineers and was elected ,to sev
eral high offices in that organization. His :
father was a pioneer and popular citizen.
He is 42 years of age. The new commis
sioner's mother and wife are residents of
this city. but he does not spend much or :
his time here. The appointment was un
known to them until a Call representative j
showed them a dispatch containing the
news of the selection. They seemed con-
siderably surprised that Mr. Meyers had *
been the one appointed, although ac- j
knowledging that he had been making ef
forts in that direction. .i*,-..-'-^
Just how close Meyers has been to labor |
is not apparent. It is said that he was
dead against unions during the big rail
road strike. Among railroad men who
sympathized with the strikers, the ap
pointment will be regarded as a distinct
slap at aggressive unionism. Meyers was
not in the city to-day. being engaged in
work above Sacramento.
This is the first appointment which the
Governor has given to San Joaquin County
in return for 8w plurality given him in the
same ballot boxes which gave Marion
de Vries. Democratic candidate for Con
gress, nearly 1500 majority. Political ex
pediency, if not appreciation, should have
led to a consideration of the opinions of
leading Republicans. As to Mr. Meyers'
executive ability or knowledge of labor
matters very little is known here. Few
knew he was even an applicant for the
office. It is said that he ls unacquainted
with Gage, but that he had a long pull ia
evident.

In Alameda County the appointment
of Meyers did not evoke popular en

gold bricks as the following comment
suggests:
OAKLAND. Aug. 28.â Governor Gage
evidently thinks that gold bricks are good
things for Alameda County. To-day he
gave absolute proof of this.
"Depend upon it. I will do something
good for Alameda County." This was the
Governor's parting remark to A. J. Read.
candidate for Labor Commissioner, two
week.- ago. after he had met the Governor
by the latter's appointment. To-day the
announcement was made that a Stockton
man had received the commission.
Three months ago it was well known
that the appointment lay between Wal
lace Pond of Berkeley and A. J. Read of
Oakland. The Governor made direct
promises that one'of these two men
should have the position. It developed
later that the man who received the plum
must be a tool of Colonel Burns. When
Mr. Read was summoned to appear before
the Governor at the Palace Hotel he was
led to believe that the commission was
about to be handed to him. Such was the
Governor's intention, but there was a rope
attached to it with as many strands as
there are clerks and deputies to be named
by the State Labor Commissioner.
A. J. Read was plainly informed that
if he was given the job he would have to
name as his assistants and deputies men
whom the Governor and Colonel Burns
had agreed upon. He was told that in so
many words, and being too Independent to
accept an honor on such dishonorable
terms he came back to Oakland.
A. J. Read had received the indorsement
of many of the labor organizations and
among his supporters were Dr. Pardee,
Victor H. Metcalf, Frank A. Leach, Col
onel John P. Irish and many other men
to whom the Governor had declared he
would give this county favorable consid
eration.
As usual, the Governor has snubbed the
whole county.
Mr. Read was asked to-night what he
thought of the Governor's appointment.
"it is a complete surprise to me," he
said. "If it were not for the assurances
originally given out by the Governor that
the commlssionership would be given to
a man from this county I would never
have been In the fight. It is true he sent
for me a couple of weeks ago and the
thing seemed to be about settled. I have
never heard of Mr. Meyers of Stockton
being a candidate for the place and cer
tainly lf he has dor.c any active work or
has presented proof of such work to thÂ«
Governor, then Mr. Gage grossly misrep
resented affairs to me when I saw horn."
Mr. Read admitted that something was
said to him about the patronage, but de
clined to enter into the exact details.
Even some of the executive's closest
friends have stated recently that the Gov
ernor had promised to appoint either
Read or Pond.
The Governor yesterday appointed
John H. Grindley and George Reed of
Oakland, and William S. Wells of Mar
tine*, directors of the Deaf and Dumb
and Blind Asylum at Berkeley.
