NOT A CANDIDATE FOR MAYOR
â¦
COLONEL DUBOCE WILL STUDY
COLONEL DUBOCE WILL STUDY
THE POLITICAL SITUATION.
Jasper McDonald Has a Heap of
Trouble on His Mind Over
Convention Af-
fairs.
The Republican local convention will not
hold a session this week, but the dele
gates of the majority may caucus to con-
The British Steamer Sikh, Now an Army Transport.
sider important topics. There has been
so much comment recently in Republican
circles in regard to the nominee for Mayor
and the name of A. B. Spreckels has been
mentioned so often ln the discussion of
probable candidates that he feels justified.
in making the definite announcement that
ho is not an aspirant for the nomination
and will not consent to the use of his
name in the convention. When spoken to
yesterday on this topic, Mr. Spreckels
said:
â¢â¢You may publish the statement as
coming from me that I am not a candi
date for Mayor and would not accept the
nomination if it were tendered to me. I
am too busy now to engage in politics and
do not want my name placed before the
convention as a candidate for any office.
I make this statement in the sincere hope
that it will end tin- discussion so far as it
relates to the proposed candidacy of my
self."
Since the return of the California Volun
teers. Colonel Victor D. Duboce has been
sounded regarding his inclinations toward
the Mayoralty of San Francisco. Colonel
Duboce* is a stanch Republican and many
of his friends believe that he could win
out if placed at the head of the Republi
can ticket. The impression exists that he
would accept the nomination should the
Republican convention display a unani
mous desire for his leadership in the ap
proaching political campaign. When the
subject was brought to his attention yes
terday he requested that discussion of the
topic be deferred until the finish of his
military service.
There is trouble in store for Jasper
McDonald, chairman of the Democratic
local nominating convention. The dele
gates placed upon him the responsibility
of naming a committee of seven to select
candidates for the Board of Supervisors.
It would be an easy thing for Mr. McDon
ald to get a list of seven from Gavin Mc-
Nab and Mayor Phelan, but the dele
gates to thÂ« convention are expressing
with considerable earnestness a desire to
be consulted in the selection of the com
mittee. Yesterday Mr. McDonald reached
the decision that a consultation with the
various delegations might result in the
submission of good material. This is cer
tainly a wise decision. Many names will
be submitted and from th- long list Gavin
McNab will surely find seven that will
stand for his slate.
In regard to forty members of the
County Committee which the chairman
is authorized to appoint, delegations will
doubtless be consulted, but no names will
go on the chosen list of forty without the
consent of Gavin McNab. These forty
added to the out-and-out slat.- supporters
already chosen by the district delegations
will give Mr. McNab the power to direct
party affairs.
The district County Committeeman or
dinarily has no higher aim in politics than
that of obtaining a little influence at the
City Hall so that Flaherty's horse and
cart may be engaged for street work, but
the committeeman at large strikes higher.
The forty to be chosen by the chairman
will shake the political tree for the rich
plums. They will direct affairs of the
party and reap the reward of rich patron
age in the event of Democratic success.
The district -gates will have a chance
to make suggestions regarding the forty
at large, but the "at largers" will be ap
pointed by Gavin Mr Nab. "The power be
hind the throne is greater than the throne
itself." __ .

A PECULIAR ACCIDENT.
Lifted in a Coal Bucket and Dropped
to Vessel's Deck.
Thomas Veahey, a laborer residing at
210 Greenwich street, in this city, met with
a peculiar accident at the end of the Oak
land pier yesterday afternoon. He was
engaged in unloading coal from the ship
Forest Hall when he became entangled in
the cable which raises the; buckets and
was lifted some distance above thÂ© deck.
After being suspended in the air for some
time he was dropped, before help could
reach him. and sustained severe injuries
of the head and body. He was conveyed
with all haste across the bay and brought
to the Harbor Receiving Hospital, where
his injuries were treateod by Dr. Robin
son. In falling the man struck directly
on the top of his head.

SEEKS TO END
HIS DAYS IN
A POOR HOUSE
Patrick Durr Deserts
the Streets.
END OF UNFORTUNATE FAMILY
FATHER AND THEEE SISTEES IN
INSANE ASYLUMS.
Â»
Son of a Wealthy Merchant Passes
Son of a Wealthy Merchant Passes
With His Kin to the Doors
of Public Insti
tutions.
t
t
The last victim of vicissitudes in the
family of John Durr, once one of the best
known merchants in San Francisco, lies
in a cell at the California-street police
station awaiting transportation to the
Almshouse. He is Patrick Durr, a son,
partially paralyzed, who has earned his
crusts of bread by begging for the past
several years. He was picked up by the
police on Montgomery street yesterday
afternoon and taken to the station, from
which place he will be sent to exist, prob
ably the remainder of his days, on public
charity. Like the others of his family
who aie now languishing in public insti
tutions, pride forces him to seclusion and
he gave to the booking officer at the sta
tion the name of Phil .ucGovern, the
name he has been known by since the tide
of fortune turned against himself and
father.
The story of the Durrs has been a sad
one. In the early days of San Francisco
the dry goods firm of Kennedy & Durr,
located on Third street, near Market, was
one of the most substantial business
houses on the coast. It held an enormous
trade and its proprietors became men of
opulence. John Durr, one of the firm,
soon dabbled in stocks. From the day he
made his first purchase on Pine street his
means began to vanish and soon he found
himself without standing in the business
community. He sought occupation as
clerk and finally passed with his family
Into social oblivion.
The nert occasion on which the public
recalled the name of Durr was November
12. â¢ 17, when The Call published a pitiable
story of destitution in a small hovel on
Valencia street. Officer Holbrook investi
gated the case and found John Durr and
his three daughters In a state of utter
destitution and starvation. They were too
proud of what had once been their lot in
life to make an appeal to charity or even
to their neighbors for assistance and bo
existed in filth and penury until their con
dition excited official attention. On No
vember 14 John Durr and his three daugh
ters-were adjudged insane and committed
to the asylum at Ukiah.
Patrick, the last of the family, has
struggled with misfortune for some time.
He drank heavily and later became para
lyzed. He has been a familiar character
on the streets, but those who met him
daily little guessed his identity. His rea
son "for concealing his name was the same
which caused his father and sisters to
face starvation in a hovelâ pride. He will
be taken to the Almshouse to-day.
â  â¦ â 
JOHN W. MACKAY HERE.
The Millionaire Mining Man Comes
to Look After His Business Affairs.
J. W. Mackay, the millionaire mining
man, arrived at the Palace last night
from Virginia City. It was reported that
he was coming for the purpose of settling
the estate of the late Mrs. James G. Fair.
As the Fair estate will not be ready for
settlement for a year or more yet this is
an error. While Mr. Mackay comes on
business connected with the estate of his
partner's widow, it is not that alone which
brings him here. His heavy mining inter
ests and other business enterprises extend
through many States, and are particular
ly extensive in this State. It is to look
after these matters, as well as to attend
to his duties as an administrator, that the
famous mining man Is now here.
Mr. Mackay is not sure how long he will
remain. Probably for several days, and
perhaps for several weeks. It will all de
pend on the length of time that it will
take him to go through his business. He
will then return either to the East or to
Virginia City. His plans are not definite
ly decided.

WILL EXHIBIT AT PARIS.
Alaska Packing Company Will Make
a Good Showing.
The Alaska Packing Company has se
cured an allotment of space at the Paris
exposition from the United States Com
mission of Fish and Fisheries and will
make a complete exhibit of its fisheries
and packing plant, together with models
showing the workings of the various de
partments of the business of the com
pany.
The exhibit will be put in at an expense
of $3000 and will show the workings of one
of the biggest Industries of the Pacific
Coast in a manner that has never been
seen before in Europe. y...- ...-

Robert Miller of the Tarn O'Shanter
â  on Trial for His Life Before a

hama.
t
The Government has chartered another
The Government has chartered another
British steamer to carry troops and sup
plies to Manila. This makes ten trans-
ports that will be ready to leave within
the next fortnight and two more are ex
pected from the Philippines that will leave
again as soon as they can be got ready.
The new transport Is the Sikh, a vessel
that has been in the Oriental trade for
nearly ten years, but came here from Yo
kohama under charter to John Rosen
feld's Sons. She was to have gone into
the coal trade, but will now be fitted out
to carry troops. The Sikh was built in
ISS9 by Wlgham. Richardson & Co. of
Newcastle, England, for the Mogul
Steamship Company. She is 1787 tons net
burden, 335 feet long, 40 feet broad and 25
feet 8 inches deep. She is a very roomy
vessel. Is fitted throughout with electricity
and will make a valuable addition to the
fleet of transports. She carries almost an
entire crew of Asiatics, but nearly all of
these will be replaced with white labor
before the steamer returns here from
Manila.
On May 2Sth last the Sikh was in New
York. From there she went to Singapore,
thence to Hongkong, Shanghai. Hioko and
Yokohama. At the latter port she was
chartered to come to San Francisco.
Of the other transports the Warren and
City of Puebla will get away this week.
The Puebla has been painted white and
looks well in her new dress. She will
probably get away this afternoon or to
morrow. The Warren has almost com
pleted loading and is scheduled to sail
Thursday. The Columbia is coaling at the
Government wharf. She will be painted
white this week. The Hancock is a; the
Union Iron Works, but her repairs are
completed and to-day she will go into the
stream and coal. The Sherman came ofT
the drydock yesterday and she also will
coal in the stream. The Centennial is
coaling at the Government wharf and the
Leelanaw is loading at Harriso-i-sLreet
wharf. Both these vessels will take sway
a load of horses. The George W. Elder
Is now at Astoria and the Belgian King at
San Diego. Both vessels will arrive Here
during the week and will be fitted tut as
transports. The Grant is now out twenty
nine days and the Sheridan nineteen days
from Manila. Both these vessels should
be on their way back to Manila by the
middle of September. The hospital ship
Relief goes to Hunter's Point drydock to
day and after an overhauling she also will
return to Manila. It was originally in
tended to send her back empty, but if the
Government finds itself pressed for ships
she may take back a load of troops. These
vessels will carry all told between 11,000
and 12,000 soldiers and all of them will be
on the way before September 20th.
An American sailor is being tried in
Yokohama for most brutal murders and
the officers and men of the steamer
Sikh are commenting upon the fairness
of the Japanese court. Robert Miller was
an A. B. on the American ship Tarn
O'Shanter. At Yokohama he fell in love
with Tonooka Suye, a Japanese, woman
who was running a saloon under the name
of George Henry, a sailor on the United
States steamer Olympia. Miller deserted
his ship and went to live in the house kept
by Tonooka Suye. He wanted her to put
the house under his name and she at flrst
consented, but finding that Miller was
drinking heavily, changed her mind.
Helping Tonooka Suye in the saloon was
a maid servant named Suzuki Aki. In love
with Suzuki was another American sailor
named Nelson Ward. Miller did not like
Ward, and when he saw the latter in the
house with Suzuki Aki the day Tonooka
Suye refused to put the saloon ln his
name he got angry. That night when
everybody was asleep Miller armed him
self with a razor and a hammer and start
ed to run amuck. He entered Ward's
room and after slashing him with the
razor beat his brains out with the ham
mer. He then went into the room of Su
zuki Aki and beat her brains out, and
then served Tonooka Suye in the same
manner. Miller then made his escape, but
was captured and was under trial for his
life before Judge Miy ajl ma Kenjlro when
the Sikh sailed from Yokohama.
The British ship King David has ar
rived at Antwerp after experiencing some
terrific weather. Her sails were blown
away, the forecastle was damaged, the
cabin flooded and everything movable on
deck washed overboard. The King David
was towed from here to Portland and
there loaded wheat for Europe. Captain
Jones was accompanied by his wife and
daughter.
The Mail Company s steamer Peru sailed
for Panama and wayports yesterday. Cap
tain Irvine of the Barracouta went out in
command, as Captain Friele is on the sick
list.
The store of Dennison. Feiling & Co.,
The store of Dennison, Feillng & Co..
205-207 Davis street, was burglarized last
Saturday night. The thieves got in
through a skylight and tried to open the
safe, but failed. They opened the cash
drawer and secured $5 40.
Attempted Murder on the Spring-burn.
There was trouble on the British ship
Springburn last Saturday night, and in
consequence there is a warrant out for
the arrest of Pat Troy on a charge of as
sault to murder. It appears that four of
the crew of the Springburn went away
without leave on Friday night and were
severely reprimanded by Chief Officer
Hiram Martin. On Saturday afternoon
the four men went to the mate and asked
for leave to see the parade, and incident
ally for some money to spend. Their re
quest was refused, and there was con
siderable grumbling in consequence. That
night at 9 o'clock, while the mate was
asleep in his room, Troy entered and be
gan beating him. Martin jumped out of
bed and Troy seized a sword that was
hanging over the mate's bed. Hearing
somebody coming, Troy ran away and
took the sword with him. Yesterday morn
ing Officer Crosbie found it hid in a wood
pile on the front and took It to the Har
bor Police Station, but no trace of Troy
was found. (

SAN JOSE AND
WATSONVILLE
TEAMS DROPPED
San Jose and Watsonville are no longer I
represented in the California Baseball !
League. The two teams, which have been |
playing ball under the auspices of the j
towns mentioned, were dropped at a meet- !
ing of the league last night. Due and de
liberate consideration was given by the
officials before action was taken in the
matter.
The meeting was called to order in the
offices of T. F. Bonnet by J. T. Moran,
president of the California League. There
were present Vice President E. F. Bert,
Secretary T. F. Bonnet and the following
club managers: Harris of San Francisco, j
Dennie of Sacramento, Tuttle of Santa j
Cruz, Ewing of Oakland and Morris of
Watsonville. . . .
Immediately upon the meeting being j
called to order Manager Harris intro
duced the subject of the condition of af
fairs in the league, referring particularly j
to the management of the San Jose club.
He explained that the club had been un- j
der the control of a Mr. La Rosa who
sold a large number of general admission
passes and then dropped out of the man
agement, taking the money with him
wnich he derived from the sale of passes.
George Borchers, he continued, had an in- ;
terest in the team and ran it along up to
May 15. when the league was notified that
owing to lack of support and the use of
the passes sold by La Rosa the new man- j
agement got no money at all. and would
have to quit if some remedy was not in
troduced immediately. Harris stated that
he and Mr. Bonnet went down to &an
Jose and induced Dr. W. F. McGraw- to
take charge of the team, and only suc
ceeded in doing so by assuring the doctor
he would be protected against any loss
he might entail. Since then the club has
beeâ¢ run at a steady loss to the Recrea
tion Park Association, owing to internal
dS" urther stated that on top of this
the Watsonville team began to fan behind
in receipts owing to the mismanagement
of Struve. And because its present man
ager Mr. Morris, wanted to be guaran
teed a certain sum for expenses, etc it
was decided to have the meeting called.
At the conclusion of the preliminary
statement of Manager Harris Cal Ew-mg
moved that San Jose's franchise be for
feited The motion was seconded by Den
nie of Sacramento and was carried unani
mously. -; Â * .. .x.,
Manager McGraw came in just at this
moment and asked that before the club
was dropped he be allowed to express his
sentiments. The motion was carried. Dr.

McGraw stated that he entered the base

HELD ON SUSPICION
F. Bisbee Tries to Elude PoliceâAl
leged to Have Committed.
Robbery.
Pending an investigation of a robbery
in which he is alleged to be Implicated,
j Fin Bisbee, a peddler, Is lodged In a cell
i of the City Prison. He was arrested last
| night by Officers Meyers and Brady and
charged with fast driving and cruelty to
j animate.
Officer Brady was standing on thÂ© cor
ner of Larkin and Grove streets last
1 night, when a boy rushed up to him and,
pointing to Bisbee and a companion, ex
j claimed that they had just robbed a man
iup the street- Brady gave chase after
i the wagon in which the men were riding
and they led him to Sixth and Market
streets. He continued down Sixth street
after the men, and believing they would
get away he blew his whistle. Officer
Meyer heard the signal and seeing thÂ©
i rapidly driven horse approaching him he
i stopped it and held its driver until Brady
came on the scene.* Blsbee's partner
! jumped from the wagon just before reach
j ing Howard street and escaped. The po-
I lice are investigating the alleged robbery.

Chinese Masonic Festival.
The festival of the Ping Kong Tr>ng. a
I Chinese Masonic Society was observed
: with boom and music in Chinatown last
! night. The affair occurs yearly and is not
jof much significance on the heathen
calendar. A large bandstand erected on
Waverly place accommodated musicians
â < and actors who donated free services and
many of the Chinese gods were reduced
to ashes in the street. The principal ob
e ject of the festival is to afford a time for
j thanksgiving and for providing for the
i wants of the poor.

THE CONGRESSIONAL OUTLOOK.
rROM interviews obtained by The Call and
the New York Herald, with members of Con-
gress, the public is given for the first time reli
able information concerning the course which will
bepursued at the coming session upon the important
questions of the Speakership of the Housf, the lead
ership of.the minority and the issues of currency
letorm and of trusts.
For some time past it has-been pretty well under
stood that despite the number of aspirants for the
Speakership, David B. Henderson of lowa would be
chosen for the position. The poll made by The Call
and the Herald now shows that Henderson's lead has
become so strong his competitors have virtually re
tired from the contest and the prospect is he will be
elected without opposition in his own party. The
Democrats are not so well agreed as to whom they
will choose for their leader. The many factions of
that party in the country have more or less represen
tation in Congress and the factional disputes there
are as notable as elsewhere. Nevertheless, the indi
cations are the leadership will be given to DeArmond
of Missouri, the party being about as tired of Bailey
of Texas as he is tired of trying to lead men who will
not be led.
One of the most satisfactory disclosures made by
the interviews is the strength of the sentiment among
Republican members in favor of grappling with the
currency problems in earnest and at once. From the
statements made it is learned the Republican Con
gressmen are virtually united in support of the cur
rency bill which will he reported by the currency
committee named by the Republican caucus. They
expect this measure to establish the gold standard
unequivocally and to increase national bank circula
tion, while breaking the "endless chain" of green
backs. Democrats from the South and West will ad
vocate free silver coinage, the withdrawal of national
bank circulation, the retention of the greenback sys
tem and the removal of the tax on State bank-notes.
On the subject of trusts the interviews arc naturally
more vague than on the other questions: That issue
has not yet been clearly defined. The introduction
of this new and powerful force in the world of indus
try and trade has caused a disturbance of business
conditions and inevitably engendered antagonisms.
Consequently, there is a good deal of clamor against
trusts, but much of it is as unreasonable as that which
was raised in a former generation against the intro
duction of machinery.
Senator Depew has expressed the conviction that
in the next Presidential campaign both parties will
declare against trusts in the national platform and
that neither would undertake to suppress them. Such
a declaration while cynical is not far from the truth.
Intelligent men of all parties recognize that some re
strictions must be placed upon trusts to prevent
abuses, but until the nature of these abuses is more
fully understood, and some definite policy in regard
to them is formulated, the issue cannot be made one
of party politics.
The people of Havana are evidently becoming
well fitted for citizenship in our glorious republic, for.
the bakers, the masons and the hackmen are on a
strike and other workingmen are inclined to follow
their example. There is nothing so easy as adapting
one's self to the ways of liberty.
The Postoffice Department has often been indebted
to the West for useful hints. Here is another: Just
outside of Kansas City, the other day. two fast mail
trains going at full speed smashed into each other.
The mail was distributed in a jiffy throughout the sur
rounding country.
The natives of Manila must be very hard to please,
for it is stated that when the American commander in
the city gave the self-styled "amigos" a chance to
show their friendliness by digging trenches and clean
ing streets under military supervision, they didn't like
it.
The future historian will write that the biggest
things brought about by the Spanish war were the
reception of the California boys on their return home
and the exposition of San Francisco as an illuminated
town.
BRYAN WEAKENING.
WHATEVER" strength" DemolrafiJ party
ever had came to it because of- its conserva
tism. lii a republic there must be government
by parties, and the Democracy for many years suc
ceeded in holding the Government by ultra conserva
tism. It was the analogue in American .politics of
the English Tories, whose modern title of "Conserva
tives" has been adopted as exactly descriptive of their
party position.
In 1896 the New Democracy changed all this and
became the representative of all forms of radicalism.
Its platform attacked at two or three points our con
stitutional system of government and threatened
ominously every safeguard of property rights. Mr.
Bryan was himself a man without property or gainful
vocation. He had practiced at a little law and a little
journalism and a good 'deal of politics. The hard
times had disemployed vast numbers of men, had
taken the profits out- of the business of another vast
contingent that had business investments, and had
sown the country with discontent which was easily
blown into flames by any leader sufficiently reckless
and restless. So many millions were pinched in their
wages, their incomes, their means of support, their
fuel, food and shelter, that no sorcery was required to
rouse them to a revolutionary pitch against all whose
prudence or good fortune had prepared them for the
crisis and put them above the hard orders of neces
sity which it brought. They were ready to elect to
the Presidency a man who professed a fellow feeling
and exploited the same needs which they felt Any
man whose prudence had prepared him to outlast the
panic was not suited to the times as a candidate of
all this discontent. In all the prosperous years pre
ceding the panic Mr. Bryan had neglected any pru
dent provision. The squalor in his affairs was not
mitigated by any share in the good things of good
times which had to be won by industry and applica
tion. In his profession, the law, all things come by
hard labor, none by luck.. Therefore, nothing came
to him. ;
He now confronts a changed situation. Our indus
tries are all in action. Crops are good. Trade is
brisk. There are but few idle who are able and will
ing to work.
As an idler and a man with no vocation Mr. Bryan
stands practically alone among the public men of
the country. His old appeals to discontent are ad
dressed to men too busy to listen. The panic has
passed like a bad dream, and the country is opposed
to bringing on another by any radical political experi
ment. Bryan has attempted to install himself in
leadership of a partisan anti-imperial issue, but his
discussion of that subject is so shallow and puerile
that it makes no appeal to the profound men who
fathom all of its depths. So he returns again and
again to the line of appeal that he used in 1896.
The people may be careless in their choice of public
officers in minor matters. They may turn a willing or
a careless ear to the solicitations of the professional
politician in choosing minor officers. But when it
comes to electing a President they don't want a man
without a calling. They have always chosen a dif
ferent class of men. Planters and farmers, profes
sional men, who can earn a living without politics,
or men who have mightily achieved for the country
and won its gratitude by great deeds and sacrifices
are the beneficiaries of the ballot when a President is
to be elected.
If Mr. Bryan had. settled down after 1806 in the
practice of his profession and had been successful.
had become known as a lawyer of respectable stand
ing and had made his living by the hard work which
success in that profession requires, he would be
stronger to-day he is.
But he did not do this. He has made his bed and
board in politics ever since, and has lived on the
sometimes generous bounty of his supporters. True,
he may have made more in this way than he could
have made by work, but after all it is a species of
charity that does not dignify him nor put him in the
great mass of his countrymen who live by work.
These considerations appeal to the keenly practical
men in his party and leave him with no whole-hearted
support, except by spoils hunters like Croker or
revolutionists and reds like Altgeld. These two
classes control the party organization and will exer
cise to the most radical limit the power to exclude
all conservatism from the convention. Mr. Bryan
will be nominated and will be again beaten, because
his impassioned appeal will be made to a country in
which' prosperity and contentment have replaced the
discontent and restlessness which were rife in 1896.
The feeling in the South is such that at this moment
ir. is probable that a majority of the Southern States
will rebuke radicalism by throwing their electo"al
votes away from him. while at the same time they
choose Democratic State Governments for the pro
tection of their local position.
There is one recourse open to General Mercier
without leaving France. He can join Guerin and
practice revolution in the seclusion of a home circle
by alternately defying the police and imploring per
mission to receive donations of bread and a leg of
mutton.
LATE PHILIPPINE REPORTS.
TWO extended reports of the Philippine situation
have recently been published. One relates to
the necessities of the military situation and its
requirements. That situation arises in the practically
unanimous devotion of the people to the cause repre
sented by Aguinaldo. It is announced that in order
to bring the people to their senses a strict coast and
inland blockade has been found necessary to cut off
food supplies, and starve into submission the non-
military supporters of the Aguinaldo Government.
j Spain always found this policy necessary in dealing
I with her disobedient subjects in the Philippines and
in Cuba. Wcyier applied it with a strictness and
severity which decimated the population, but even
then did not succeed. The resources of Spain and
her power to make such a policy successful did not
equal ours, and there is no doubt that we can,' riot
only continue to prevail as we have in the field opera
tions against those people, but that we can strike
! them far more fatally and effectively by starvation.
They are a dense population. They are apparently
united and will fight as long as they can feed. They
have a deadly climate as their ajly. When we use
starvation as ours, we have the odds by reason of our
superior strength and inexhaustible military re-'
sources. The island of Cuba has only a million and
a half of people and it was estimated that Weyler
had disabled more than half of them by starvation
and had by that means destroyed the lives of a quarter
of a million. That we can do better than this in the
Philippines is the well-settled opinion of our military
authorities, as shown by the dispatches referred to.
The other report is on a subject collateral to the
foregoing, i It is to the effect that members of the
Peace Commission held elections for Mayor in sev
eral cities on Luzon. Under their authority the peo
ple of San Pedro Macate, Baliuag, Imus and some
other cities elected Mayors, who took charge of the
municipal governments and their revenues. In every
case it was found that the voters had chosen active
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALI,, TUESDAY^ AUGUST 29, 1899.
sympathizers with Aguinaldo, who at once proceeded
to use their- power and revenues in his interest. To
stop* this 'they all had to be arrested by our military
-authorities and put in jail in Manila. It is said their
trial for treason will follow and we suppose they will
be shot, as an admonition to their successors. These
events show the deep seated support of Aguinaldo
by those people. The cities named. are within our lines,
where no threats nor terrorism by Aguinaldo could
reach them. They were sure of our military protec
tion against him and yet they proved to be as thor
oughly subject to his influence and in sympathy with
his purposes as if they were within his lines and
reachable by his vengeance. The devastation of their
fields and the effective application of the policy, of
rigid starvation seems to be all that is left to our
military authorities to reach their case.
The last dispatch commenting on this says that
these occurrences have ended all ideas of leniency
among the Americans. Their patience has evapor
ated as Weyler's did, and they are fully prepared to
put in force the only plan that he ever found effective.
The dense population renders this easier than it looks.
The food resources of tropical countries are not good.
The work in Philippine fields is mostly done by
Chinese, and the exclusion order of General Otis,
forbidding Chinese to come, is a severe blow in line
with the new policy of reducing the population by
starvation and using hunger to humble the spirit of
the survivors.
On the sentimental side of it, the argument is prop
erly made that starvation as a part of our military
j tactics cannot increase our unpopularity among the
i people. They are practically unanimous against us
I now, and their sentiments cannot be changed by
j anything we do or leave undone. When men of the
j high scholastic standing held' by the members of
I our Peace Commission conduct elections among
i them and they persist in electing the candidates who
j agree with the native aspirations, it is plain that they
j are insusceptible to any high appreciation of the pur
i pose for which they are permitted to vote at all.
The committee of one hundred job chasers is hav
ing such a good time in the Democratic convention,
it has almost forgotten that the chasing of the jobs
will have to be done before the public and that the
job is a long way ahead and running like a deer.
GOOD reports come from Sacramento to the
effect that the prospects of the State Fair are
exceptionally bright and promising. In the
spacious pavilion every inch of the floor space has
been taken by exhibitors with the understanding that
their booths must possess artistic merit and that in
their displays nothing commonplace will be allowed.
It is particularly gratifying to note that entries
already made show that the exhibits of California
products in all departments of the pavilion display
will be large, varied and of a high class. Such ex
hibits will be sure to attract the particular attention
of all visitors, whether they be Californians or persons
from other States. The chief object of the fair is to
afford an opportunity for annual exhibits of the pro
gress of the industrial development of the State in
all branches, and as a consequence the dominant
thought in the mind of an intelligent student of the
exhibit will be to sec how. far that development has
gone, and what it has accomplished in the way of
providing for all the needs of civilization.
There is no reason why the State Fair should not
attain on the Pacific Coast such eminence in the way
of an annual exposition as to attract and hold the
attention of people in all parts of the country. It
should be an exhibit at which men can see each year
what has been accomplished in their lines of indus
try, what improvements have been made in imple
ments and machinery and what results achieved by
new methods of work whether it be agricultural, me
chanical or artistic.
The Directors of the State Agricultural Society
have this year made earnest efforts to awaken an
interest in the Fair among all classes of citizens and
in all localities. They have offered strong induce
ments for county exhibits and for the display of the
products of cities. The result of their efforts will be
but partially disclosed this year, for the impetus given
now will go over to the succeeding year and tend to
make the exhibits of 1900 even better than those of
this season. The outlook, therefore, is promising not
only for the present but for the future, and all Cali
fornians may well be gratified with the prospect.
If the French had been wise in the management of
attractive sensations they would have kept the Drey
fus trial for the exposition year and held it in the
biggest hall in Paris.
SUPREMACY IN THE TRANSVAAL.
ONE by one the minor points in the controversy
between the Boers and the British have been
eliminated and the crisis is now reached on the
single issue of which of the two Governments has
paramount authority over the Transvaal.
According to late reports President Kruger and
the Boer Council are willing to concede everything
the British Commissioner has demanded, but couple
the concession with the condition that it be recog
nized as a concession only and that Great Britain
abrogate all her claims of suzerainty over the repub
lic. On the other hand, the Salisbury Government
is more persistent in asserting suzerainty than in de
manding redress for the alleged wrongs done to the
Outlanders, and the issue is thus brought to a point
that undoubtedly portends war.
In a speech delivered at Birmingham on Saturday,
Mr. Chamberlain declared the situation to be threat
ening and condemned President Kruger for pro
crastinating and for making offers accompanied by
conditions whicii he knows will not be accepted. The
speaker went on to say the action of the Boers com
pel the British to strengthen their force in South
Africa and make ready for w3r. He added:
"If this delay continues much longer, we shall not
hold .ourselves limited by what we have already
effered, but having taken this matter in hand we wili
not let go until 'we have secured conditions which,
once for all, will establish us as the paramount power
in South Africa and secure for us our objects there â
equal rights and privileges promised by President
Kruger when the Transvaal's independence was
granted.
It appears we are about to witness another- scene
of that long drama in which the weak peoples are
crushed to make room for aggressive ambitions of
the strong. The little nations are being swallowed
up by the "benevolent assimilation" of the big nations
with imperial missions to civilize the world and estab
lish liberty through the power of the heavier guns and
the larger battalions. The Boers, however, are going
to be a tough morsel for even so voracious a lion as
that of Her Britannic Majesty to swallow. Kruger's
procrastination has not been a waste of time. He has
been arming his brave Boers for a final struggle for
the independence of their country, and it may be that
through his indomitable courage the Transvaal, may'
stand in Africa like. Switzerland in Europe â home
of unconquered and unconquerable freemen.

How He Rose From a Lawyer's Clerk to Be
One. of the Greatest Powers in

THE LATE JUDGE HILTON.
JUDGE HENRY HILTON, who died
last Thursday In New York, became
conspicuous through his connection
with the estate of Alexander Stewart,
the dry goods millionaire.
The first dozen years of Hilton's life
have always been hidden in mystery. His
parentage, tha exact place of his birth
and even the date of his birth are un-
known. He would never tell of them.
But from the best information obtain-
able the ex-Judge was born in New York
city about seventy-five years ago, and
it ls said his father was a carter. The
first thing definitely known is that he be-
came a clerk ln the law office of Camp
-1 bell & Cleveland in Grand street, when
he was about 15 years old.
An Indefatigable worker, he became
managing clerk qf the firm and was such
when Alexander Turnly Stewart, the man
who had come penniless from Ireland and
a client of the firm, was being talked
about as a very successful business man.
Stewart's business was gradually out-
growing his store at Broadway and
Chambers street, and he was planning to
build a great white marble structure that
would be the greatest store in the coun-
He frequently consulted the law firm on
his plans. The lawyers turned them over
in a great measure to the managing clerk
and soon Stewart began consulting direct
with the subordinate. The big structure
which Stewart erected was in great part
the result of young Hilton's advice.
A few years later Hilton resigned every-
thing else to become private counsel and
confidential secretary of the. great mer-
chant prince. He soon became acquainted
with every detail of the vast business. He
counseled Stewart to move uptown. The

A GREAT SACRIFICE.
There was one way and one way only
out of the difficulty that had arisen.
Stewart should give up the business with
which his name had been Identified for
more than forty years and become a re-
tired merchant. It was a tremendous sac-
rifice to make. The annual profits of the
great stores, retail and wholesale, ran
Into the millionsâ somewhere from two to
four or ten millions of that gold of which
Stewart, in his all-devouring avaricious-
ness, could never get enough. Was it un-
der the advice of Hilton that he consented
to the sacrifice and surrender of It all?
No one now can tell except Hilton him-
self, and it is very certain that the secret
will never be disclosed by him-.
The "Judge" was playing for great
stakes at that time. Whichever way he
turned there were millions in full view-
millions on which, subsequent events show
he had got his eyes, eyes more avaricious
than even those of Stewart himself.
Twenty-five or thirty- thousand dollars a
year was paid about this time to "advise"
his master, and what was the advice?
That this ambitious old man, 67 years of
age, should assign the interest of'his mil-
lions to him and accept the petty salary*
of 18000 a year as a Cabinet officer with
in the future. Robeson and Belknap and
Williams as his associates. It was a mag-
nificent play for millions on the part of
Hilton. The date was 1569. in the early
March days of that year. If fortune had
not played Henry Hilton false then he
would have been in the saddle of the
Stewart millions at a date seven year*
anterior to that on which he vaulted into
their control, after the old man's eves
had been closed forever on Cabinet "of-
fices, on coin and on cotton, on hotels
and on hosiery, on earth and all that
earth contained.
When Stewart died he left Hilton $1,000,
- But in some wav that has never been
I explained nearly all of the Stewart mil
! lions came into Hilton's possession. He
became the owner of the store at Broad-
way and Ninth street. He changed the
firm to that of Hilton. Hughes & Den-
ning. But the money making power had
vanished with Stewart and Hilton's plans
i never prospered. The business failed
finally in 1596.
After Mrs. Stewart's death numerous
efforts were made by relatives to deprive
Hilton of the Stewart millions. All failed.
Judge Hilton had much trouble in the
last few years with some of his sons, who
were inclined to be wild.

RENEWED INTEREST IN THE
GREAT MONTEREY EXCURSION
A Fine Programme of Sports Prom-
ised by the Mayor.
Now that our citizens have somewhat
recovered from the excitement attend
ant on the magnificent demonstration
accorded to our returning California
volunteer?, the public's attention is
now being gradually manifested to
ward the great Monterey excursion
which is to take place Sunday next,
September 3.
The Southern Pacific passenger de
partment are just in receipt of a letter
from Mayor Johnson of Monterey, from
which we quote the following:
"Various committee* who have this
programme of sports in hand report
everything working well. We will have
about twelve to fifteen of the worst
'broncos' that can be found in this part
of the country and which will amuse
the people when they are saddled by
good riders. Will also have about the
same number of wild two and three
year olds. A few wild buffaloes will
also be on the ground for the bare-back
riders. Work on the grand stand will
begin Monday." y2- -'. '>.v*:
As heretofore announced the excur
sion trains will leave Third and Town-
Bend street depot at 8 o'clock a. m., and
returning will leave Monterey at 4:45
p. m. These trains will run through
â without stops, except for water and
registering.
Special excursion tickets will be
placed on sale at the Grand Hotel
ticket office on Wednesday, the 30th
Inst., and continued up to Saturday in
clusive. Tickets will be on sale at
Third street depot and Valencia street
on the morning of the excursion.
Mr. William H. Menton, excursion
passenger agent, reports a large inquiry
for these excursion tickets, the low
rate of $2 being well received.
Oakland passengers will take the
train which leaves East Oakland at 6:30
a. m. Passengers from Berkeley will
have to take the 6 a. m. train and those
from Alameda will take the local train
leaving Park street at 5:55 a. m.
It should be remembered that this is
the last excursion of the season to Del
Monte, Monterey and Pacific Grove at
the J2 rate.

SENSATION IN
PARRY CASE
IS EXPLODED
Defense Explains to
Prosecution.
Matters moved slowly in the Parry case
in Judge Graham's court yesterday after
noon. Attorney Knight and the defendant
kept the court waiting for over a half
hour but when they finally appeared
apologies were offered and accepted ami
the case went on. , Z'*'Jl
There was the promise of spicy develop
ments when Prosecuting Attorney Went
worth introduced testimony to prove that
Mrs Parry had registered at the Palace
under the name of Mrs. Corbett. Specta
tors leaned forward and there was an
awful hush in the courtroom. Then At
torney Knight, with a winning smile and
easy grace stepped to the front and with
one fell swoop smashed the sensation.
â¢'There is no necessity for all this ques
tioning," he said. "We admit Mrs. Parry |
registered at the Palace under the name
of Mrs. Corbett. 1 do not know whether
it was as Mrs. Jim. but I do know it
was done at her husband's request and
that afterward he was ejected from the
hotel because he, Mr. Frank Parry, was
'found in 'Mrs. Corbett's room.

who was put on the stand by the prose

Waiters Have a Grievance.
The Walters' Union has a grievance
against Caterer Abrams, who served and
furnished the breakfast and banquet to
the California volunteers. The members
of the union who assisted Abrams on both
occasions allege that he agreed to pay
them $2 50 per day for their services and
only gave them $2. The matter will be
brought up at the next meeting of the
union. - - *

THE SAN I KAN CISCO CALL, TUESDAY, AUGUST 29, 1899.
DUKE IS ASSIGNED
TO CHINESE QUARTER
Sergeant Donovan and his squad have
been relieved from duty in Chinatown.
Yesterday Chief Lees, on account of the
temporary Illness of Donovan, decided to
withdraw the Chinatown squad. Ser
geant Duke of the O'Farrell street station
is to take his place Duke's squad will
consist of Policemen Robert Conner. John ;
Herlihy. "John Davids, John Conlan and i
Fabian Callahan.
Sergeant Donovan, notwithstanding
that he has been detailed in Chinatown
only a comparatively short time, has
made a brilliant record. Ably assisted
by Police Officers Campbell, Save, Lyons,
Hering ar.d Griffin, he succeeded In clos
ing a number of games that were con
sidered impregnable by the former squads.
In his anxiety to suppress gambling In
Chinatown .Donovan overworked himself,
and .is the result he became ill. Chief
Lees, on learning of his condition, ordered
him to stay away from the Chinese quar
ter until he became convalescent. Despite
the order of the head of the department
Donovan, evidently anxious about his
men, frequently visited Chinatown "just
to see what was going on," as he cx
i pressed it. Realizing that his impaired
i health would not allow him to continue
in his determined fight against the law
i breakers of the Chinese quarter. Chief
i Lees decided to withdraw him and his
squad.
Sergeant Duke was selected by the
Chief to take Donovan's place, and at his
j suggestion he was allowed to pick the men
c that compose his squad.
Donovan to-day leaves on a two weeks'
'. vacation. On his return he will be as
i signed to duty at the O'Farrell street sta
| tion to take the place of Sergeant Duke.

CORPORATIONS SUBJECT
TO THE STATE LAW
United States Circuit Judge Morrow yes
terday handed down an important opinion
in favor of the respondents In the suit of
Frederick Williams vs. The Gold Hill
Mining Company, George C. Gaylord,
Charles E. Maddrell and Dwight T. Rolfe.
The action "nad teen brought to fore
close a mortgage of $50,000 on one hundred
bonds of the denomination of $300 each,
issued July 1, 1860, interest payable semi
annually at the rate of 10 per cent per an
num. The mortgage was held by G. Liv
ingstone Morse of New York as trustee.
At the trial it was contended by the re
spondents that the mortgage was void for
the reason that it had been Incurred with
out a two-thirds vote of the stockholders,
as required by the laws of the State of
California. The complainants set up the
contention that the Gold Hill Mining Com
pany, being a foreign corporation, was
not bound by the laws of California. In
his opinion sustaining tiu jurisdiction of
the laws of the State over foreign cor
porations doing business within its boun
daries Judge M.ii row said:
"The personnel and the Internal man
agement of foreign corporations are sub
ject to the laws of the State In which
they are organized, and by virtue of
which they are entitled to exist as cor
porations. But when such foreign cor
porations come to do business in Califor
nia they are restrained by the public pol
icy of the State from the enjoyment of
privileges not participated In by the do
mestic corporations organized under and
existing by virtue of the laws of this
State."
The opinion goes on to say that the laws
of the State prescribe that mortgages
must be ratified by a two-thirds vote of
the stockholders, and the mortgage upon
which the cause of action was based not
having been so ratified is Invalid.

The Coroner's jury that heard the testi
mony at the inquest yesterday morning
upon the bodies of Deputy Sheriff James
B. Brown and Mrs. Fannie Barnes re
turned a verdict charging George Tanner
with murder in the first degree. Coroner
Hill disapproved of the verdict, and upon
his instructing the jury that a verdict of
murder was all that was necessary under
"Crowner's Quest" law, -the verdict was
changed to that of murder.
Tanner was present at the inquest,
guarded by Detective Dillon. Ke de
clined to make any statement. He is a
short, thickset man and looks every inch
the sailor with his grizzled beard and
brown skin.
The first witness was Louis Mill, pro
prietor of the St. Malo lodging house, in
which the shooting was done. He swore
that Tanner was drinking heavily last
Saturday and that he had had an alter-
cation with Brown downtown, and that
Brown had slapped his face. When Tan-
ncr saw Mrs. Barries in the hallway he
called her a vile name and she struck
him across the face with a towel that

volver which he had given to him two
months before for safe keeping, l.pon

William Phillips, the next witness, was
not a witness to the shooting, but after
Tanner's arrest the witness asked Tan
ner why he had killed Brown and Mrs.
Barnes." and Tanner replied: "1 had to
Mrs Phillips, wife of the preceding wit
ness had a talk with Tanner at about
half 'past 2 o'clock in the afternoon, and
took "just one drink" with Tanner out of
a quart bottle of whisky that Tanner had.
He was under the Influence of liquor and
told her that he had been to the jail to
see Brown and to inform him of Mrs.
Barnes' conduct, he having heard that
the woman had been In a back room of
the Hoffman saloon with some man or
men. He and Brown went to the Hoff
man that night to watch for her in case
she should go there again.
When Brown and the woman came
I home, a short time before the tragedy,
I Brown spoke of having struck Tanner In
the face when they had met downtown.
Mrs Barnes also spoke of the trouble
she had had that -morning with Tanner,
when she slapped his face.
At tho time of the shooting Mrs. Phil
lips was ln her room and did not see the
Robert Whittle, detective, testified that
when he arrested Tanner immediately
after the murder Tanner was drunk, and
said that he did the killing in self-de
fense. Tanner seemed to be sorry He
was not so drunk that he didn't know
i what he was doing.
The prisoner was taken before Police
I Judge Conlan and was instructed as to
his rights. His preliminary examination
was set for September 1.

SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver lower. Financial quotations unchanged.
Wheat futures weaker.' Spot prices unchanged.
Barley continues quiet at previous prices.
Oats, Corn and Kye about the same.
Nothing new in Beans and Seeds.
Hay depressed under continued heavy receipts.
Potatoes and Onions weak. Vegetables plentiful.
Three ears of Eastern Poultry at hand.
Eggs firm and several descriptions advanced.
Butter and Cheese show no change.
Another advance in Bartlett Pears. Peaches dull.
Dried Fruits show no change.
Provisions quiet at previous prices.
Nothing new in the Meat market.

EASTERN MARKETS
New York Stock Market.
NEW YORK, Aug. 28.â There was a marked
falling off of interest in railroad stocks to-
day compared with that prevailing last week.
Interest shifted Into the Industrial specialties.
and a number of these were marked up in a
feverish and Irregular manner. The genera]
list was maintained with difficulty and a slight
flurry in call money in the last hour carried
prices, with the few exceptions of strength, p
below last night's level. In the final harden-
ing movement on covering by shorts, some of
*
the specialties' and a few obscure railroad
stocks rose to t'.p level. This made the clos-
ing of the market linn. but the majority of
standard stocks show net losses. An influ
ential factor In tlic hesitating and doubtful
tune and speculation was the renewed anxiety
ever the meney market outlook caused by Sat-
urday's bank statement; Call money was quite
stiffly held at 3 per cent all day and was
marked above that in the last hour, possibly
not Without manipulation. But the fact re-
mains that the present low stage of surplus
reserves of he New York Clearing House banks
keeps the financial world keenly observant and
in a somewhat uneasy frame of mind. Pro-
fessions pf confidence that the West is amply
provided with funds to nance the coming crop
movement without calling Oil New York, do
not altogether avail in the face of tie- con-
dition of the local banks. Neither does the
Hucgestion that the Peal companies have large
available funds which do not figure in clearing
liouse banking reserves, though both of these
explanations liave undoubted force. The buy-
ing for London account, which has served to
sustain some stocks recently, was missed. There
were upward movements of importance to-day
in the Tobacco stocks, Manhattan, Metropoli-
tan. Steel; Rail was, -Sugar and some of ill"
In en and steel stexks. the latter under the in-
fluence of a declaration < .f the first dividend on
the Republic Steel preferred of 1% per cent.
called quarterly. American Express jumped 10
points over Saturday's, price. On the other j
hand there was a weakness in International j
Paper, Cotorado Fuel. Brooklyn Transit, Ten- |
nessee Coal and a few other specialties. In the j
railway list the only stocks to stiow any marked
demand were Southern Railway preferred and
Kansas and Texas preferred.
Kansas and Texas seconds are 3% higher on
the day. Otherwise there was no notable feat-
ure in "th<: bond market. Total sales, par value,

Chicago Grain Market.
[ CHICAGO. Aug. 28.â For a good part of the
morning wheat was firm at a slight advance in
prices. Liverpool showed hut a small opening
decline and more than recovered this before
the close there. A more important considera
j tion was the Â«mall world's shipments and more
\ particularly those from Russia, whose large
exports last week were an important factor in
causing a downward turn to the market. Rus
sia this week reported shipments of 499.000
bushels, the total world's shipments being
5.739,000 bushels. Northwest receipts were
somewhat larger, but not enough so to bear-
lshly affect the market. Shorts were the best
buyers at the opening . Northwest and other
outside markets were weaker than this and
some pressure was put on the market by pro
fessionals and elevator people for this reason,
which caused a temporary decline. September
opened %c to %c higher at 79% c, and declined
to 70% c. December opened V' to %c higher at I
72%Cg7;'c, an.! eased off to 72%372%c. The de- I
cline was stopped by the statement that the !
visible supply had decreased 1,517,000 bushels, !
and the slackening in the selling pressure com- j
ing at a. time when shorts were covering, prices !
advanced again. September getting to 70%671c
and December to 73c. These proved the high
points of the day. A period of _ comparative
Inactivity followed, but before noon the market
turned decidedly weak on the reported arrival
of 300 cars of new wheat at Duluth and by *
much more favorable thrashing returns from
the Northwest. Elevator people and profes- I
sional bears were not slow to take advantage I
of this and pounded the market persistently c
from then until the end of the session. De- ;
cember declined to 71% c and September to 70c. j
There was a tendency on the parts of shorts j
to cover at those figures and a slight rally
occurred, but the general feeling was weak
to the close. The seaboard reported a . large
export business in the break, but the cash
market was i>oor. September closed at 70Â®
70%e. and December at 72c bid.
Corn was weak for September, but steady for j
December and May. Crip reports continued \
favorable. September closed %c lower, De- :
cember closed %c lower. The speculative mar- |
ket for oats was almost at a standstill. Sep- 1
tember closed unchanged; December closed %c j
lower. â â¢â¢'
The market for provisions was firm but dull. j

of ribs, which led the firmness in the specula- ',
tive market. Packers were good buyers of lard
and ribs. At the close September pork was
unchanged, lard and ribs 7%c higher.

Northern Wheat Market.
Northern Wheat Market.
OREGON.
PORTLAND. Or., Aug. There -was but
little change in the local wheat market to-day.
Walla Walla can be bought at 680 for new
wheat and 69c for old. Offerings are very light.
Valley is about 59080 c, Bluestem 61c. Flour Is
higher. Reports from the interior in regard
to the crop damage, while still conflicting, are
much more favorable and In a great many sec
tions the less In winter wheat will be made up
by the Improved condition of late sown spring
grain, which utilized all of the moisture to ad
vantage, in the Willamette Valley there is
considerable bleached wheat and some has
sprouted, but with careful handling and no
further damage it Is estimated that at least
75 per cent of the crop will be saved, 6ome of
it possibly in an inferior condition.

Wheat and Other Grains.
Wheat and Other Grains.
WHEATâ The spot market was unchanged,
but futures were weaker.
The Chicago market opened firmer under
better foreign advices, small world's receipts
and shipments and a decrease of 1,500,000
bushels In the visible supply. Broomhall
cabled that the political situation was affect-
ing prices. The Roumanian crop was estimated
at 50 per cent of last year's. Seventy loads
were taken for 'export.
Later on the market weakened off in sym
pathy with weakness In the Northwestern
markets. The hulls became discouraged and
began to let go and the market closed dull
and weak.

Hay and Feedstuff s.
Receipts of Hay were 1299 tons. The market
was naturally weak, but the demand was fair
and prices showed no further decline. The cir
cular of George Morrow *& Co. says: "The
market Is duller than it has been for a long
time. The average yearly receipts from all
sources do not exceed 120,000 tons. During the
past four weeks we have received at this point
18.172 tons or three-tenths of the year's supply.
Or putting It In another way we have received
in one month almost two months' supply of
hay Not only this but the worst ts yet to
come. We do not say this because we are
alarmists or have the slightest desire to de
press the market, but simply because lt
stands to reason that the early rains may very
shortly be expected and there will be a very
natural desire to get the hay out of the fields
as soon as possible. Even as It is, if schoon
ers were not so very scarce, the receipts would
be far in excess of what they are now. It
would seem to be good policy to either store
or else ship to market as soon as possible."

THE STOCK MARKET.
Local securities were quiet in the morning'
and prices showed no variation, worthy of note.
In the. afternoon there was some activity in
th" sugar stocks. Hawaiian Commercial sold
down to $95 50 and Giant Powder sold up to
$74 87%. '
Mining stocks continued quiet, but the feel
ing was firm and several showed a slight ad
vance. The 'telegram from the pump said:
"At 6:30 this morning the water was thirty
four feet below the 1950-foot level station. No.
1 elevator has been running continuously."
The Justice delinquent sale takes place to
day.
In the Sierra Nevada mine, on the 140 level,
the south drift has been extended 'twenty-two
feet; total length 332 feet. The face is ln clay
and porphyry. East crosscut No. 3 has been
extended sixteen feet; total length seventy
two feet. The face is ip east clay and the
crosscut has been stoped. 900-foot levelâ West
crosscut No. 7 from the north lateral drift is
in thirty-six feet. The face is in porphyry.
The official report from the Consolidated < 'ali
fornia and Virginia mine lor the past week is
as follows: 1400 levelâ The west drift has been
advanced through porphyry, seams of clay and
quartz a distance of eighteen feet: total length.
836 feet. 1650 levelâ No work has been done
during the week. 1750 levelâ ln the old upraise,
mentioned In previous reports, we have put in
three sets on the second floor and raised one
the third floor and put in one set north on
the third floor. We have extracted from the
third floor fourteen cars of ore, assaying 567 23
per ton gold. * On the eighth floor in the stopes
we have put in one set "south and one Bet
south on the seventh floor. Have extracted
from a small streak on the sixth floor one car
of ore, assaying 875 34 per ton in gold. Have
saved from the seventh and eighth floors thirty
five cars of fillings, assaying $11 97 per ton in
gold. 1800 levelâ West crosscut No. 1. from the
south drift, eighty 'feet from the winze sta
tion, lias been extended thirty-one feet; total
length, eighty-three feet. The formation passed
through consisted of hard porphyry and hunches
of quartz and clay seams. The south drift
has been cleaned out thirty feet through mate
rial giving assays of from $3 to $'"> per ton:
total length, 235 feet. 1950 level-Have finished
the repairs on the station. Have cleaned out
and put in a floor in the west drift for a dis

The official letter from the Ophir mine for
the past week says: Jointly with the Mexican
Company we have been making repairs in the

tral tunnelâ Joint west crosscut has been

necting drift has been extended twenty-five

length, fifty-five feet. The face is in quartz,
porphyry and clay, giving low assays. In the

a formation of quartz, porphyry and clay. In

Special Dispatch to The Call.
WOODLAND, Aug. 28.- The late Walter
Root was committed to the Agnews Asy
lum for the Insane last December. He
died on Friday. August 25. The news of
lits death came as a complete surprise to
his relatives and friends, as a letter had
been received a few days prior to the'an
nouncement containing Information to the
effect that he was improving, both men
tally and physically.
The body was brought to Woodland last
Friday evening, and all those who viewed
it expressed surprise and indignation at
its condition, which plainly Indicated
neglect and lack of attention upon the
part of somebady at the asylum.
The muscles and ligaments, as well as
the soft tissues of the left elbow, were
broken, and the elbow Joint showed indi
cations of having been wrenched apart.
There was nothing bout the Joint but a
plain cotton bandage.
T. B. Gibson, a brother-in-law of de-
T. a brother-in-law of de
ceased, informed a Call representative
that Dr. Dillon, another brother-in-law,
made an examination which disclosed the
fact that two ribs had recently been
broken on the left side of the body, but
had so knitted that the injury "could
scarcely be noticed upon a superficial ex
amination.
The features were distorted, the mouth I
and eyes open and the expression of the I
face indicated that the prisoner died dur- i
ing violent exertion. As the insanity of
the patient during most "1" his confinement
was of a violent form it is possible, and !
even probable, that these evidences of vio- j
lence were self-inflicted, but it seems as- ;
rounding that no effort was made to close
the eyes and mouth and that the body
was shipped away from the asylum in a I
. â¢ p.dition indicating brutal neglect.
The relatives of the deceased and mem
bers of the Masonic fraternity are so |
worked up over the matter that there is |
some talk of demanding an investigation. I

THE SCIENTIFIC WORK OF
A FOOD EXPERT.
It Is a plain enough proposition that.
lt Is a plain enough proposition that.
if we use up, destroy and cast out
minute parts of the body each day, we
must make good these parts and re-
build each day or we go backwards
and break down.
Chemists have analyzed the little
particles thrown off through the pores
of the body and And Phosphate of
Potash as the result of active brain
work.
This must be replaced or nervous
prostration Bets in.
Can we rebuild it with the drug Phos-
phate of Potash?
No, for nature demands that element
furnished as found in the grains, In
fruits and in meat, and as prepared in
her own accurate laboratory.
Pood experts have utilized the knowl-
edge that brain matter is composed of
Albumen and Phosphate of Potash.
They have taken the parts of grains
known to contain these elements and
skillfully prepared a food for human
use, giving it the name of Grape-Nuts.
This food is of delicious flavor, is in
coarse granules and ready cooked. It
can be served instantly with a little
cream or milk, hot or cold, as V pre-
red.
A distinct increase in mental power
is observable after ten days or two
weeka use of Grape-Nuts.

Clay Clement in His
Old Success.
-'Â : â â  â * ...-'*: . -
Comparison of "The New Dominion"
with "A Southern Gentleman" sets one
i thinkingâ and the thought hangs a por-
tent. What direction did criticism of the
former take last year that Mr. Clement
! should, have selected the qualities least
admirable in it. and in the latter enlarge
I upon them so as to bend and hinder his
work as a dramatist? I mean the tend-
ency to wordiness, to long speeches and
the introduction of the vocabulary and
\ subject matter of verse. The speech about
j the human flower is excellent â most in-
teresting, but it has no dramatic value.
and this sort of thing is pushed to the
limit in "A Southern Gentleman." It is
the stuff out of which one makes wooden
passionless plays. Over-thought for pret-
tiness Induces nausea, and while a play
j may get along perfectly without esthetic
qualities, lameness of action, lack of
i honest appeal to human emotion, will In
! evitably weaken it. Provided it be not
i grewsome. considerable downright blunt
I ugliness is a good dramatic qualityâ it is
j so human. If Mr. Clement means to keep
on spreading this veneer so generously he
! will ultimately cover his strength and
I present only the insipidâ the colorless.
The South seems to be the Mecca of all
1 v.ho would write character plays and
] catch that Intangible something called
i "atmosphere" of wonderful value, be-
cause see elusiveâ elusive as the Zeit-Gelst.
The theme is over-handled, the subject
: about threshed to the limit, and yet Mr.
Clement has given us in "Tin New Do-
minion" a play of quiet, even interest,
i subdued in situation, natural in key,
witty in dialogue, carrying its strength
not so much in -its plot, which is not novel
; (the "Old Homestead" mortgage Idea),
as in its well-drawn characters, chief of
which are Hohenstauffen and Marshall
Boner and Martha. Hohenstauffen Is a
CrOSS between a type and an eccentric,
but with a comfortable leaning toward
the former. He Is a slightly exaggerated
composite of a large German class, but
done with as thorough a knowledge of
values as a Nicholson portrait or a Phil
May sketch, and it would be a caviling
critic who would not tell Mr. Clement
that he has cleverly drawn a most Inter-
esting character, and acted it to the life.
Not -a cloud hangs over his German dia
lect and the insight with which he ap
preciates his own Idiomatic breaks is
quite as humorous as the breaks them-
selves. The substratum of the hard, un
! flinching Teuton, eve:* visible through the
surface polish, is a bit of ballast that
moors the Baron Franz Victor securely
to this earth. Otherwise one might
vaguely fancy that heaven was his only
worthy home. Marshall Boner is not a
unique species of brute. He is the old
story of the double inheritanceâ money
and an injudicious palate In whisk
and is portrayed by Mr. Jeffrey Williams
with thoroughly hateful, convincing
strength. He is to be congratulated on
his choice of cravat so., potent a thine
in revealing a man's breedingâ it stamped
the vulgarity of his character at onco.
Of the others, save Miss O'Malley as
Martha and Mr. O'Malley as Napoleon,
the negro, one would like to write a
colorless paragraph committing himself
to nothing, for one's thoughts in regard
! to them are neutralized between satisfac
j tion and the opposite. They do not mar,
| neither do they enhance the value of the
play. Hohenstauffen alone is well worth
i spending an evening with.
CHARLOTTE THOMPSON.
Tivoli.
Ponchielli being one of the modern mas-
Ponchlelll being one of the modern mas-
ters, "La Glaconda" Is replete with that
Intense passion which makes dramatic
singing such a grateful task. Consider-
ing; the fact that last night's performance
at the Tivoli was a first night, and but
scanty preparation could be made for its
production, it must be admitted that It
was beyond doubt a creditable perform- '
ance. Of course, Avedano. as usual, re-
ceived the largest share of the applause,
which he endeavored to earn by consci
entious work. As to the excellent quality
of his robust tenor there is no denial, but
as to the quality of his deportment much
adverse criticism could be jotted down.'
The title role was in the hands of Anna
Lichter, where it fared very well. "Many
remarks were made as to the poor method
of Miss Lichter's singing, it is a great
mistake to assume that this vocalist's
method is faulty. On the contrary, she
sings as true as steel, and never fails to
strike the correct note. She may be af
flicted with a slight vibrato, but in this
she does not stand alone. The fact is that
Miss Lichter is a conscientious worker
and an Industrious vocalist, upon whom
the management may depend at all times,
and this s Imuch more than '.in be said of
other, more "illustrious" members of the
company.
William Mertena was obliged to study
the English libretto of Barnaba at two
hours' notice, owing to the sudden indis-
position of Salassa. This is the second
time Salassa became suddenly indisposed,
much to the discomfort of the manage-
ment. Sickness is sometimes convenient
to serve as an excuse for appearing two
nights in succession. None of the other
singers are afflicted in this manner. Mr.
Mertens deserves great credit for his gen-
erosity and also for his quick study. Sure-
ly his work last night did not exhibit any
signs of a hurried preparation.

rific paces.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, AUGUST 29, 1899.
TAX LEVY IS
MAINTAINED BY
SUPERVISORS
Dollar Limit Not Sent
Skyward.
Yesterday's meeting of the Board of
Supervisors was productive of something
considerable in the line of surprises; it
was a day for the short-enders; the tal
ent is still guessing. None of the unex
pected things transpired without a
wrangle; the Republican majority had all
it could do to block the persistent at-
tempts of the Democratic minority to
make Democratic politics out of about
every situation that presented itself ani
a good bit of heated discussion was the
I result of it all. i.yP
Mayor Phelan started matters off nicely
by descending from his accustomed alti
tude and. acknowledging that he had got
off decidedly wrong in his tax levy fig-
ures; then Supervisor Ferrault's motion
to reconsider the vote whereby the tax
levy was reconsidered was carried, and
, then Supervisor Deasy flopped again; he
i reconsidered the motion he made last
week to break into the dollar limit for
i the benefit of the Sixth street sewer and
thereafter voted against any proposition
that might lead to an abrogation of the
tax levy as established by the Finance
Committee.
Incidental to the other startling devel
opments of the day the gas company sent
[in Attorney Thomas B. Bishop .say that
Mr. Crockett and the rest of them would
hereafter keep within the provisions of
the one-twelfth act and render bills for
lighting accordingly; but the tax levy was
the one prominent feature of the meeting.
A goodly audience had gathered in the
chambers to see the levy inflated and
started skyward by those who had made
it, but it failed to go up despite the earn-
est efforts of Mayor Phelan, and the audi-
ence went to the door to demand its
money back.
The first order of business was Per-
rault's motion to reconsider, but before
demanding it he moved that when the
board adjourned it do so to September 18.
His motion was seconded by Holland and
carried with only a couple of dissenting
votes. Then he called up his motion for
reconsideration, and no debate ensuing
Clerk Kusseli opened up with the roll-call.
Aigeltinger, Attridge, Black and BJ-ing-
ton all voted to reconsider and the votft
was up to Collins when it struck Mayor
Phelan that the/board had decided not to
break through the limit* after all and see-
Ing a clever bit of Democratic politics be-
ing voted out of his hands he immediately
interrupted the roll.
< "The members of this board do not seem
to know what they are voting for," ex-
claimed he, and then he hastily attempt-
ed to explain the motion. Supervisor
Ferrault Jumped to his feet and demanded
the rollcall proceed from Collins on, and
said that Mayor phelan had no right to
Interrupt a rollcall for political or any
other purposes. The Mayor murmured
his customary excuses and ordered the
rollcall to proceed, and the motion was
carried by a vote of Bto 4. Then Perrault
moved to lay the original motion to re-
consider on the table, and that motion
also was carried. Aigeltinger, Attridge
and Collins voting no.
With this last indorsement of the dollar
limit Supervisor Deasy's Sixth-street
sewer hopes went a glimmering. It was
in order to have the street fund increased
$10,000 for vote-making purposes south of
Sixth street that he made the original
motion to bolt the pledge and the main-
tenance of the levy knocked that game
in the head The board, however, was
met uncharitable and offered him consola
tion in the shape of a gold brick resolu
tion which provided that the Street De-
partment fund be increased $10,000 to be
used for the specific purpose of repair-
ing the Sixth-street sewer. It was point-
ed OUt that as the tax levy question had
been disposed of that action could pot be
taken, so the board adopted only the last
two paragraphs of the resolution, which
read as follows:
Resolved. That the City Engineer is hereby
required to make all necessary examinations
and report to the Superintendent of Streets the
condition '.f the sewer in Sixth street, between
Folsom and Harrison, and connecting sewers
requiring reconstruction, as heretofore ordered.
Resolved, That the Superintendent of Streets
be and he hereby is empowered and required to
proceed forthwith and make provision for re
constructing said sewers, ,to abate the nuisance
now existing.
Perrault questioned that Auditor Wells
would audit bills for the work if they ex
ceeded the one-twelfth act, and moved
that in be called in consultation. When
asked he said he would strain a point in
favor of the Sixth-street sewer, as he
considered the imjrrovement a matter of
emergency and one demanded by the
whole people In the district affected.
By a resolution Introduced by Super
visor Holland Architects Shea & Shea
were Instructed to take possession of the
Hall of Justice for the city if Bateman
Bros., the contractors, shall not have
finished the structure by September 1.
The resolution further provided that
Bateman Bros, should be lined 15000 as a
penalty tor non-fulfillment of their con
tract, and that the "building shall be
completed by the city out of funds as yet
unpaid tec the contractors. The resolution
was adopted, Black and Phelps voting
no. As Bateman Bros, acknowledge they
cannot. finish their work by the appointed
date,, the Hall of Justice will be taken
into the hands of the city on September 2.
Although the tax levy had been dis
posed of. President Bergerot of the
Board of Education, with two black
boards, and Superintendent Webster
broke in at this point to argue for a
larger appropriation for his department.
President Bergerot was also accompanied
by several misstatements of considerable
magnitude, which he offered the board as
solid fact. He said in opening that the
average salary of the school teacher in
San Francisco was $â¢", per month, and in
doing so he ignored the fact that the
books of ids department show that the
average salary is $78 per month with a
couple of months' vacation thrown in. He
said also that the last Legislature passed
a law which provided that a teachers'
salary . fund should be created and that
the amount expended for teachers' sal
aries during one year should be the figure
upon which the fund of the succeeding
year should be based. Mr. Bergerot said
that this amount this year should be
(1,088,640, but he neglected to say that in
that figure he had not only Included
teachers' salaries but janitors' pay, oflice
and shop salaries and rents as well, all
of which the law of th." !ast Legislature
did not lake into consideration nor pro
vide for. :V
- Bergerot also said that the same
law provided that if the Supervisors
should not establish such a fund and fix
this figure, as specified, the Auditor him
self should add it to the tax levy. He
told the same thing to Auditor Wells
earlier in the afternoon, and Mr. Wells
smilingly denied the constitutionality of
the alleged law and declared his intention
of ignoring it. The Auditor said that in
creating the teachers' fund the Legisla
ture was establishing a class of preferred
creditors against the School Department,
and that the constitution did not acknowl
edge such a class. He further said that no
law in the land gave him the power to in
any way alter the tax levy as fixed by
the Supervisors, and that until there was
a law he would not do so. Despite the
Auditor's decision Mr. Bergerot handed
all of these points out to the board in
support of his effort to break into the
limit, and when he had finished Mayor
Phelan came to his support with the
statement that he had discovered that in
presenting the figures upon which the
board had fixed the School Department
appropriation he had been guilty of a mis
calculation. He asked permission of the
board to present another estimate, and
Supervisor Perrault got up to say that on
behalf of the board he would agree to
adopt any figures the Mayor might sug
gest. ' *â  *â â 
"Why do you not move to adopt any tax
levy I may suggest?" asked Mayor Phe
lan.
"I make that motion," promptly retort
ed Perrault, but when it came to a vote
no one cared to take the chance, and the
motion failed of adoption.
Supervisor Black's resolution to repeal
the anti-pool selling order next came up,
and, as was stated would be the case in
yesterday morning's Call, further action
upon it was indefinitely postponed. .

resolution, which was presented by Super

man, a nephew, is named as a possible
executor.
