MR. AND MRS. DANIELLS
ARE AGAIN UNITED
Parade the Corridors of the Hotel
Rafael "With Arms
Linked.
SAN RAFAEL, Aug. 28.â The dove of
peace seems to have at last perched over
the house of the Danlells and the Hast
ings , and with the chronicling of the
event is written the final chapter of a case
of domestic infelicity, celebrated in Amer
ica and Europe on account of the prom
inence and social position of the principals
and the wealth of their families. Mrs.
James Daniells and her husband have evi
dently determined to carve out their own
fate in the future, despite the protests of
relatives.
The titled descendant of a family re
lated to the celebrated Beau Brummel and
his" wife were in a happy mood to-night,
' and marched through the corridors of the
! Hotel Rafael arm in arm, with Mrs. Dan-
iells' two little children clinging to them
ami prattling in childish glee.
Mrs. Daniells was formerly Miss Mamie
Coghlll, and her marriage to Robert Hast
ings was a celebrated event in the social
world. At his death she and their two
children inherited a large fortune. She
afterward married. James Daniells. and
went to London to reside. She was' there
seven years when tales of domestic strife
became whispered about. Early last spring
Mrs. Daniells returned to San Francisco
and stayed .at the Palace Hotel until
a few months ago. when she took up her
residence at the Hotel Rafael. Many tales
were told of her separation from her hus
band, one being that the relatives of both
parties were dissatisfied with the mar-
riage, and another being that the wife of
the Rev. Dr. Hemphill, the children's ma
ternal grandmother, discovered that Dan-
iells had squandered his wife's inherit
ance and was using that of her grand
children, a condition of affairs which
caused her to have the children brought
back here.
Last Saturday Daniells came to San Ra-
Last Saturday Daniells came to San Ra-
fael and found that his wife was in San
Francisco. This evening he returned and
registered at the Hotel Rafael, taking
apartments across the hallway from his
wife Later the couple wandered about
the hotel with arms linked, and the chil
dren expressed their joy to several that
"Uncle Jim" and mamma had made up.
By a whim of Mrs. Daniells the children
had always called their stepfather "un
cle." .
NEW POSTAL ORDERS.
The Postmaster General has advised
Postmaster Montague of the adoption of
a new form of postal money order which
will be introduced gradually as the sup
ply of the old forms become exhausted
after September 4. .;--
The new order will be about the size of
an ordinary bank draft. The advice or
notification to be sent by the issuing to
the paying Postmaster is a reproduction
of the order through the use of carbonized
paper (the manifold process) and a receipt
for the amount will be furnished to the
remitter by the issuing paymaster. ,
By the carbon process the order and
advice are produced simultaneously. By
the same operation the essential particu
lars of name of payee, date, amount and
place of payment as written In the order
are duplicated. The liability to error is
lessened and absolute uniformity in
insured between the order and advice.

PASTOR PLAYS THE
PART OF PEACEMAKER
OAKLAND, -Aug. 28.â Miss Jane Hodge
had bought* her new bonnet, her tailor-
made dress and all the accompanying
articles that go to make up a bridal
trousseau when John Nieholl, the aged
capitalist, told her "it is all off."
The testimony that is gradually coming
out in this case breathes very strongly .
of a little plotting here and there to re- j
lieve the ordinary romance of love-mak- j
ing. In her deposition, which has just
j been taken. Miss Hodge told Mr. Nicholl's
! attorney, A. J.- Mhoon. of all her prepa
! rations for her marriage. The hat was
I ordered in Oakland, the tailor-made dress
i was created in San Francisco, and many
I other articles were procured in various ;
I places as the lady happened to see them j
i when completing her wedding outfit.
! The rides she took with her aged lover .
1 also occupied a large part of Miss Hodge's j
: time while giving Tier deposition. It was j
during one of these rides that Mr. Nieholl
| ventured to put his arm around the lady's ;
; waist and to murmur sweet words, which :
i have since laid the basis for this inter- |
esting damage suit. December 10 of last '
! year was a very interesting time in Miss ;
I Hodge's life. On that day she was at
I home with her aunt when Mr. Nieholl '
I drove to the house. In a spirit of fun j
Miss Hodge said to aunt: "Why don't j
\ you go driving with Mr. Nieholl?" to |
i which the aunt replied: "Go on, you silly :
' thing. You know he has come for you. j
Go and put on your wraps." "Yes. go |
'-. and put on your wraps," said Mr. Nieholl. '
I Then followed the ride, and from all that
j can be learned the very Interesting cere-
mony of poppU.g the question was re-
peated.
Attorney Mhoon was very anxious to i
obtain from the lady her opinion as to
what this attention on the part of Mr. j
Nieholl might mean, his attentions, being
apparently to make it appear that the
relationship was more like that of father
and child than of lovers. The lady quick- :
1 lv disabused the attorney's mind, and left !
j on record a very clear impression as to i
i how she interpreted Mr. Nicholl's attitude j
j and words. '
Mr. Nieholl wrote to Miss Hodge quite
1 a long letter, and it contains evidence :
'. that there was a promise of marriage and c
that it had a string to it. Mr. Nieholl in j
i this letter says: "You remembei that we i
! were to be married if my daughters did
not object to having a new mamma. Now
! they have objected, and of course, as you
' say, the marriage is all off, and I hope
our friendly relations will be the same as |
I they ever were."
Since the suit commenced a minister
has entered on the scene. Rev. Dr. Curry
of the Temescal Presbyterian Church, who ,
i used to be the pastor of a church for- i
! merly attended by the Nieholl family, â 
i called on Miss Hodge to make a pastoral :
visit. Miss Hodge was rather surprised â .
1 and does not quite understand why a min
ister who is not her pastor should come '
'to talk to her about her love affair. Dr.
1 Curry expatiated to Miss Hodge about
the beautiful spirit shown by the Savior
when he forgave his enemies Â»nd how
'â â  noble it was to forgive our enemies. He
referred to the trouble "in the family" ;
. which had been brought about, as he in
timated, by Miss Hodge. When he got
thus far the lady took issue with her
reverend visitor and told him that she
had no enemies; that Mr. Nieholl and his
daughters had never asked her to forgive
them, and wound up by telling Rev. Dr.
Curry that the Nieholl family had brought
about all the trouble by denying her en- j
gagement with John Nieholl and by rush
: ing after the reporters and giving the de
tails of the incident in the newspapers.
Miss Hodge says that she supposed Dr.
Curry called on her in a religious way, :
1 but she is strongly of the opinion that
there was also another motive to his visit.
She declares, however, that she resented :
i the Interferences of any third party ex- j
cept attorneys between her and Mr.
Nieholl and that the minister went away |
: without obtaining from her any inside
secrets relating to her dealings with Mr.
! Nieholl.
Miss Jane Hodge Is very anxious for
I the trial to be commenced. She says she
I would not have brought such action ex
cept for the purpose of establishing her
character. Several times hints have been j
' thrown out, she declares, by the Nieholl
family that she is somewhat of an ad
venturess or else losing her reason. These
insinuations she indignantly resents and
refers to the fact that she has been known
' to the Nieholl family for forty years and
i that therefore there can be no foundation
! for the suggestion that she is either a
schemer or crazy.
The completed deposition will be read
over by Miss Hodge and signed next
Wednesday.

Suicide at Marysville.
MARYSVILLE. Aug. 28.â A stranger
who registered at the Golden Eagle Hotel
on Saturday evening as Harvey Breaze
died in his room last night from the ef
fects of a large dose of morphine taken
with suicidal intent. To. the druggist who
sold him the poison he represented that
it was for a man named John Lapp. De
ceased spoke ; with a | French accent, and
his age is about 60 years. His belongings
indicate that poverty forced him to the
rash act. The Coroner cannot locate any
acquaintances here.
OVERWORK IN A CANNERY
ROBBED HIM OF REASON
.... Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
908 Broadway, Aug. 28.
90S Broadway, Aug. 28.
Robbed at birth of his powers of speech
and hearing and now bereft of his reason
and hearing and now bereft of his reason
through overwork at the San Leandro
cannery, Lon Ben Jackson, aged 24 years,
may spend the rest of his days in an in
may spend the rest of his days in an in
sane asylum.
His case is one of the saddest that has
His case is one of the saddest that has
been brought to the Receiving Hospital in
many a day, and his examination before
the Lunacy Commissioners has been set
for to-morrow morning.
for to-morrow morning.
Young Jackson has been residing with
his' mother at Seminary Park. His father,
D. Jackson, is the owner of the old Half
way House on the County road, and ls
traveling in the East as a liquor drummer
at present. Lately young Jackson lias
been working at the cannery in San Le
andro and it is supposed that -the irksome
labors deranged his mind. He began
showing symptoms of a falling mind last
Saturday and these grew more pro
nounced yesterday and to-day, until he
lost all control of himself. East night he
arose in his nightclothes and climbed up
to a windmill to stop it. In his vagaries
of fright over imagined impending dan
gers he grabbed his aged mother this
morning and refused to let go. Neighbors
were summoned, but all attempts to
break the maniac's grasp proved futile.
The mother's earnest and piteous plead
ings were idly spent on deaf ears, and
finally Sheriff Rogers was notified.
Through some ruse Deputy Sheriffs
Striker and Wales eventually induced
young Jackson to accompany his mother
to the carriage that awaited his transpor
tation, and as soon as he stepped into the
vehicle the deputies overpowered him.
strapped him down and hurried him off to
the hospital. There on a slip of paper ho
wrote: "I am sorry to make my mother
lie." and following something about fruit
destroyed and his being an American.
The unfortunate young man has a sis
ter, who was also born deaf and dumb.
and who is at present an Inmate of the
Deaf and Dumb and Blind Institute at
Berkeley.

FELL FROM A HIGH ROOF
TO INSTANT DEATH
Oakland Offlce San Francisco Call
90S Broadway, Aug. 28.
Abe Leach, a shingler, fell from the roof
Abe Leach, a shingler, fell from the roof
of the Newland House this morning and
was killed. In his fall he struck the edge
of the roof of a building lower than the
one on which he was working and was
undoubtedly fatally injured before he
undoubtedly fatally injured before he
reached the ground.
reached the ground.
With another carpenter named Morgan
Leach was working on the roof of the
Leach was working on the roof of the
Newland Ilouse at Seventh and Washing-
Newland House at Seventh and Washing
ton streets. He was carrying a bundle of
shingles and failed to notice the tele
shingles and failed to notice the tele
phone wires attached to the roof at the
very edge of the building. His foot
caught in the wire, he lost his balance
and fell. His head struck the metal cov
ering of the roof beneath and this de
flected his fall, throwing his legs through
a window of the saloon beneath as he
turned over in reaching the sidewalk.
John Morgan was the only witness to
his companion's death. He said Leach
was just four feet away from him, carry
ing a bundle of shingles from the top of
the ladder to the place where he (Leach)
was at work. Morgan turned around just
in time to see Leach's foot caught by the
wire, and a second later he heard him
striking below. He says the man uttered
no sound and that the accident was all
over in an instant.
Contractor Howling was working with
the men on the roof and had just spoken
to Leach. A moment later he heard, a
crash and on looking around missed the
shingler, when Morgan exclaimed, "He is
gone." Contractor Dowling says Leach
had worked for him for twenty years and
was a very good workman. About ten
years ago his wife lost her reason and
attempted to murder him. She is now in
an asylum, and Leach was frequently
very melancholy over her misfortune.
The dead man has no relatives so far
as known and his remains were taken to
the Morgue.

SAYS SON IS UNFIT
TO BE A GUARDIAN
Charles Krytser's Mother Opposes
His Appointment to Care for
His Appointment to Care for
His Eather.
His Eather.
OAKLAND, Aug. 28.â Elizabeth Kryt-
ser to-day entered vigoroifs objections to
the granting of the petition of her son,
Charles J Krytser, wherein the latter
asks to be' appointed guardian of the per
son and estate of his father, John Kryt-
Mrs Krytser denies that the mind of her
husband has become Impaired from the
! effects of ill health or at all, or that he
has ever been incapable of, taking care of
; himself. She denies also that the son is
the only relative residing in this county,
' and avers that she herself had been ter
n'â â  porarily absent from her home, 1202
! Eighth street, visiting relatives in lowa.
i She declares that the son is not a fit and
I proper person to be appointed guardian,
i and that his sole purpose in filing the
petition is to obtain possession for his own
i use and benefit of the father's property.
! Hence she asks the petition be denied.
A lively contest is expected when the
! matter comes up for hearing, since the
son claims his purpose to be to recover
valuable property his father deeded to
I the mother while he was sick and infirm.
The Catholic Mission.
' BERKELEY, Aug. 28.â Father Mac-
Corry, the brilliant young Paulist who has
been conducting the services of the Cath
olic mission in St. Joseph's Church on
Addison street, delivered his farewell ad
dress this evening. Father MacCorry
leaves shortly upon an extended mission
tour to Montana. The services of the mis
sion in Berkeley will be continued by
Father Doherty. who accompanied Gen
eral Merritt to Manila and labored among
the soldiers there. Father Doherty will
continue to officiate until the end of the
week.
Mark Anthony Died Intestate.
OAKLAND. Aug. 2S.â Melinda Anthony
to-day applied for .letters of administra
tion on the estate of her husband, Mark
Anthony, the Inventor, who died intestate
in Boston on the 7th Inst. While the ex
act value of the estate is not known the
petition recites that the household furni
ture in the Oakland home is worth $200;
that the deceased had $1767 on deposit in
the Second National Bank of New York
City, $1859 in the Golden Gate Manufac
turing Company of New York and $100 In
a Hartford, Conn., bank. The heirs are
the widow, three daughters and 'wo sons.
Boat Crew Returns.
BERKELEY, Aug. 28.â The members of
the University of California crew which
took part in the regatta held last week
at Astoria, Or., returned to Berkeley to
day. They brought with them as a
trophy the silver cup given for the
junior four-oared barge race. The trophy
will be placed in the university library
with the names of the crew engraved
on lt. All the men say that the trip up
to Astoria unfitted them for doing their
best work. Tracy, who went as stroke,
and who was too sick to enter the races,
is reported as better.

J. O. Scott has come up from Agnews
and is registered at the California,

Is at the Occidental, accompanied by his
wife.
F. P. Wickersham. the Fresno capital-
ist, is among the arrivals of yesterday at
the Lick. ,
A. Wcilheimer, a prominent citizen and
leading merchant of Fresno, is a guest at
the Grand. ZX y
John F. Cat-rare has come down from
his home In Sacramento and is staying at
the Palace, '.yiZX /-i
J. Marion Brooks, one of the best known
of the lawyers of Los Angeles, is a guest
at the Grand.
Garrison Turner, a wealthy land owner
of Modesto, is at the Lick. Miss Turner
accompanies her father.
J. W. Taggart, an attorney of Santa
Barbara, is a guest at the Grand, where
he arrived yesterday morning. .
John C. Knopp, one of the recently ar-
rived Idaho delegation, is at the Occi-
dental, waiting for the arrival of the
Grant. ' . PS.iiZZrix'Z.X 'xP. 'â ''^'iv.
E. W. Johnson, "who has come from
Idaho to bid the boys of his State wel-
come when they return, is staying at the
Palace.
Edgar P. Pray and A. E. Peake, two
belated members of the -North Dakota
delegation, arrived in the city yesterday
and registered at the Palace.
W. H. Haw. County Clerk and ex-officio
clerk of the Superior Court and Board of
Supervisors of Humboldt County, Is in
town. Mr. Haw is a bright young Re-
publican politician. .'â¢''. P-
Eugene F. Burt and Andrew J. Clunie
returned on Sunday from the six weeks'
trip they have been making throughout
the East. 'They thoroughly enjoyed their
journey and have been much rested by
the vacation. â¢ *
Jacob Goldberg has returned from
Europe to New York and ls stopping at
the Hotel Savoy. Mrs. Goldberg and her
son, Sol Goldberg, start Tuesday evening
for New York, where they will spend a
couple of months. '
J. C. Franks, formerly United States
Marshal, is at the Waldeck Sanitarium
recovering from a severe surgical opera-
tion for the removal of a calcareous
growth from his right knee joint. He will
be out in a few days. p. P
Mrs. Josephine M. Garonne is now on
her way to Paris to visit her sister, Mrs.
Victor Masson. Mrs. Garonne is accom-
panied by her niece, Miss Ruth Forbes,
who goes to receive her education In one
of the seminaries of Europe.
Frank A. Coffin of Boise, Idaho, Is a
guest at the Occidental. He has come to
the city for the purpose of meeting the
Grant and being one of the first to greet
the volunteers of his State who are re-
turning -on her. :-*'*y
Lieutenant Governor Joseph Hutchinson
of Idaho is at the Grand, accompanied by
his wife and daughter.. He comes to join
the Governor of Idaho and the j rest of
the representative citizens of that State
who are here waiting to welcome their
returning volunteers. ;V -; Â C-V.
A very pretty little compliment was
paid to the California boys the other
night through Lieutenant Curzons . of
Company L. While the troops were seat
ed at the supper in the nave of the ferry
building a messenger brought in a box
to the lieutenant, who, opening It, drew
forth a floral wreath. It had followed
the regiment.all the way from Honolulu
and was sent by Mrs.. John Ena with an
"Aloha nui" to. the officer, in the hope
that while he was enjoying the hospital
ity of his native city' he would not forget
the people who had entertained his regi
ment in the Paradise of the Paciflc.

MINDLER'S BAD BREAK.
Judge Mogan Allowed Him to Go on
Probation, but Y*ssterday He
Was Drunk in Court.
A. W. Mind ler was arrested several
days ago for. malicious mischief and dis
turbing the peace. He went into Mrs.
E. Hart's "restaurant at 513 V& Jones street
and destroyed nearly all the , crockery.
When he appeared before Judge Mogan
he pleaded that he was drunk and was
sorry for what he had done and prom- I
ised to defray the cost of replacing the j
crockery. The -Judge allowed him to go
on probation and continued the case till i
yesterday.- - â¢ - ,
Mindler appeared in court yesterday.
but he was very drunk and Officer Cough-. ;
lin had to hold him up while the Judge
lectured him. He denied that he was
drunk, which incensed the Judge,- who
curtly asked: "Do you want your sen
tence now?" Mindler tried to straighten
himself up, and in a husky voice said: |
"Do as you please, Judge; it's up to you." :
The Judge ordered him taken to prison !
and a charge of drunk booked against j
him. and when he appears this morning
be will get the full limit on both charges, i

VOLUNTEERS WILL BE
WELL PROVIDED FOR
Employment Committee Under-
takes Practical Work.
Over Forty Thousand Dollars Will Be
Applied to the Care of Sick and
Needy- Boston Goes to Mare
Island at Once.
NOW that the glamor and the. glare.
the hurrahing and the exuberance
of the welcome to the soldiers have
passed away the members of ' the
committees that represented the people
of the State in the celebration are getting
their heads together and closing up their
accounts. The balances will be struck
to-day at meetings of the executive and
finance committees. It is estimated that
the people have contributed about $65,000
in round figures for the reception and
care of the returned soldiers. The ex
penses of the celebration approximate
$22,000, which will leave a balance exceed-
ing $40,000 to be applied to alleviating the
sufferings of the sick and succoring the
deserving destitute and needy.
With the passing of the committees that
raised the fnuds and arranged the wel
come the work of the committee that has
undertaken the task of securing employr
ment for the volunteeers fairly begins.
The members are working with a will,
appreciating that theirs is the practical
portion of the whole affair.
Rabbi Voorsangor, who is the chairman
of the committee, is devoting much of his
time to the undertaking and is enthusi
astic ln the work. He is on hand at the
headquarters tent every afternoon, plan-
ning the committee's campaign and lend-
ing what aid he possibly can to the sol-
dier applicants for work.
The committee met yesterday afternoon
and had as its guests Mrs. A. S. Towns-
end and Miss Rattlgan. Rabbi Vexjrsan-
ger introduced Mrs. Townsend to the
committeemen as the "soldiers' fairy ;
godmother." Sho believes that the com-.
mittee's work is the most practical of any
that has been undertaken in the Interests
of the soldiers and volunteered her finan
cial assistance if it was needed.
Many encouraging reports were received j
and from indications there will be no
difficulty In securing employment for
every member of the California Regiment '
who wants to work. C. E. Whitney & Co.,
wholesalers, Bent notification that several
of their men left their employ to enter
the service of their country and thai .as
Boon as they are mustered out they could
return to their old positions.
J. D. Smith of Livermore advised the j
committee that his section of the country
-could supply employment for the whole
regiment in the hop fields and the fruit
orchards. The Sanger Lumber Company
offered permanent employment to sixty *
of the California volunteers at $1 75 a.
day, at places where board could be had
for $15 a month. These offers were en- j
thusiastically received by the committee.
Babbi Voorsanger stated thai a great
number of mustered-out men of other i
States were remaining in San Franciso
and would eventually develop Into '.nee of
th.- great problems with which the com
mittee would have to deal. It was the
sentiment that the Californians should
be provided for before anything could be i
done for the strangers.
Colonel Duboce has expressed his strong
approval of the committee's work and has
Assured them of his hearty co-operation. ]
By his Invitation the members will con j
Bult with him next Friday afternoon at !
regimental headquarters at the Presidio
and arrange for getting a statement of
the condition of every man in the regi- !
ment and his desires as to the future.
Many of the soldiers have already reg
lstereo their applications for work at the
headquarters tent, and among these are
several who apply for positions as bank [
clerks, or are willing to accept any situa
tion the duties of which are not more i
arduous, provided the remuneration is
fully as large.
The efforts being exerted for the re
turned volunteers has suggested the ques
Tion. What is to be 'lone for the families
or relatives of those who will never re
turn â have yielded up their lives for
their -country?
The Red Cross expects to hear of many
.cas-es of destitution among those whose
â providers lost their lives in the Philip
pines. Nothing is being done in the way
of locating such cases or offering relief.
The Red Cross is of the impression that
â¢they are beyond its jurisdiction, but will j
certainly not allow any case of want that i
Js brought to its attention to pass without
receiving the proper attention.
A requisition for $500 will be made on the :
*??ecutlve committee to-day to prosecute
the work of the employment board. The i
latter body has named C. S. Benedict of

RAILROAD ESTIMATES
NUMBER OF VISITORS
!. According to the statement of G. F.
' Richardson, master of transportation of
the Southern Pacific, that company alone
; brought in to this city 145.000 people to
' I witness the festivities of last week. Of
this number 93,000 arrived over the broad-
gauge, 40,000 over the narrow-gauge and
the coast division brought no less that 12.
--! 000. This count is based on the actual
number of tickets sold and does not in
-1 elude the thousands who arrived before
; the commencement of the week and those
brought here by other lines, which should
swell the total to something like 250,000.
Every one of these joined the entire pop-
ulation on the streets to witness the night
parade.
Traffic Manager McCormick of the
> Southern Paciflc Company denies the re-
cently published report that his company-
has refused to recognize ' the tickets of
j the Rio Grande 'Western and the Colorado
Midland over its lines. Mr. McCormick
Bays that this city is the only place where
tickets over those two lines are not Issued
and that Is because of an agreement en-
tered into with the traffic men of those
roads, which have not yet completed their
end -if it here. Tickets are Issued at Los
Angeles and are honored wherever they
are presented. Mr. McCormick says the
Los Angeles statement is completely un- j
authorized and that he cannot understand
whence it emanated.
HARBOR COMMISSIONERS
RETURN THEIR THANKS
The Board of Harbor Commissioners
has issued the following in acknowledg
ment of courtesies extended in arrang
ing the day celebration:
Referring to the glorious reception to the
California Volunteers, the Board of State Har
; bor Commissi desires to acknowledge the
. receipt of favors and return Its thanks for the
prompt and generous assistance rendered in
carrying out that part of the. demonstrations
delegated to It by the executive committee.

assistance, we are particularly indebted to

IDAHO'S SENATOR ARRIVES.
George L. Shoup, the State's Senior
Representative, Awaits the Grant.
George L. Shoup, United States Senator
from Idaho, arrived at the Grand last
evening to take his place with the rest of
the Idaho delegation in the welcome they
will extend to their State's volunteers
when the transport Grant pokes her nose
in through the Golden Gate. As the Grant
Is now expected to arrive at almost any
moment he will not have long to wait be
tore he has an opportunity to greet the
fellows who have done so much to entend
the glory of the.State he represents.
When seen last night Senator Shoup
said that as yet no decided preparations
had been made for the reception of the
volunteers In Idaho other than those al
ready published. The people were more
than willing to do anything In their pow
er to honor the lads who had sacrificed
so much to their love of country, but they
preferred waiting until the boys them
selves come' home and have an opportu
nity to say what they want.
As far as the Santor himself is .con
cerned, he says he is willing to stand In
to the extent of his pile, either in money
or influence, in any and all plans that may
be suggested.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, AUGUST 29, 1899.

CALIFORNIA OFFICERS
RECEIVE PROMOTIONS
Promotions came thick and fast among
| the California men yesterday. There
\ were six in the First Regiment and five
' in the artillery, and there are more to
come.
Major Boxton of the first battalion of
the First Regiment was made lieutenant
colonel; First Lieutenant Curzon, regi
mental adjutant, was made a captain;
Second Lieutenant Adler of Company L
was raised to a flrst lieutenant; Regimen
tal Sergeant Major Badt was maoe a sec
ond lieutenant in Company L; Sergeant
Dowdell of Company tl was made sec
ond lieutenant of his company and First
Sergeant Swan of Company D was made
second lieutenant of his company.
In the artillery battalion First Lieu
tenant J. W. F. Diss of Battery D was
made captain of that battery to succeed
Captain Steere; Second Lieutenant George
L. McKeeby was made a flrst lieutenant
ln Diss' place and First Sergeant Frank
Willard was commissioned a second lieu
tenant in McKei by's place; Second Lieu
tenant A. P. Hayne of Battery A was
made a flrst lieutenant in place of J. B.
Morse, who died in Manila, and First Ser
geant D. J. K-eohane was commissioned
second lieutenant in Haynes' place.
All the commissions date back from the
time the vacancies whicj they fill oc
curred. They are issued by the State,
under the order of the President, which
allows the Governors of the various* Slates
to appoint all officers below the grade of
colonel.
The commissions were presented to the
officers yesterday morning in the presence
of the adjutant general and several mem
bers of the Governor's staff.
There Is a well defined rumor among
the men of the regiment to the effect that
September 21 will be the date for muster-
Ing out.. No date has been set. however.
but if the work be properly attended to
somewhere about the -Ist will be the
time. Yesterday afternoon four companies
had so far recovered from the celebra
tion as to make a good start on the work
of the mustering out papers. The others
will start in earnest this morning.

RUSHING RECRUITS
TO THE ISLANDS
A New Troop of Cavalry for the Pre-
sidio â The Case of Assistant
Surgeon Isaac P. Ware.
More recruits are being prepared at the
Presidio for transportation to Manila. The
Puebla will sail to-day with 650. The War
ren will leave on September 1 with 1200
and the Columbia will leave about Sep
tember 5 with 800 more. This will practi
cally clean cut the camp of casuals now
at the Presidio, and it will probably In
clude one regiment of the four now on
their way here from the East. The first
of these is expected here in two or three
days, and the others will not be long in
following. It was the intention to keep
them here for a month anyway and drill
them in open order tactics and on the rifle
ranges, but it may be that part of them
will be sent out on one of the three trans
ports.
Dr. John J. Gil h uiey, who has for some
time been connected with the general hos
pital at the Presidio, will go out with the
Warren as surgeon in charge. He has been
looking for some time for a chance to get
to Manila, and he says if he can make the
trade with one of the surgeons down there
he will leave the ship when she arrives
and will remain with the hospital service
of the Eighth Army Corps. .
A retiring board met In the ' headquar
ters yesterday to pass upon the mental
capacity of Assistant Surgeon I. P. Ware.
Captain Ware has been on the sick list
ever since he attended the smallpox pa
tient quarantinued at the Presidio some
months ago, and for a long time he has
been unable to attend to his duties. The
retiring board consists of Major General
W. R. Shatter, president; Colonel J. B.
Babcock, adjutant general; Colonel Mor

Troop X of the Sixth Cavalry has been
ordered to the Presidio from Fort Riley,
Kansas. It will take the place of Troop
F. Part of Troop F has been on patrol
duty at Yosemite National Park for a
month past, and yesterday the remainder
of the troop left for the same place.
The men of the Colorado regiment
turned over their arms to the post ord
nance officer yesterday. There now re
mains for the regiment but to await the
action of the mustering out officers upon
the regiment's papers, and then for the
men to settle their accounts and leave f6r
home. A special train will take them back
to Denver. I -#
In a circular from headquarters atten
tion is called to the fact that the names
of all legally qualified enlisted men who
desire to appear before a board of officers
for examination for appointment as sec
ond lieutenants in the army, must be at
once reported to headquarters. The ex
aminations are held in the fall of every
year.

BOSTON ORDERED
TO MARE ISLAND
From the present outlook the proposed
reception to the officers and, crew of the
Boston will not take place. It was ar
ranged for Wednesday night of this week,
when â  Market , street would again .be il
luminated, this time in honor of the men
who fought with Dewey in the memor
able Mayday engagement.
It is now officially announced that the
Boston is ordered to proceed to Mare Isl
and this morning. The executive' com
mittee, of which Mayor Phelan is the
head, has been preparing for a royal re
ception, but unless present orders are
countermanded there will be no theater
party for the officers and jackies of the
Boston. Mayor Phelan has telegraphed
Rear Admiral Kautz at Mare Island ask
ing him as a special favor to allow the
Boston to remain in port until after
Wednesday night.
A telegram was received from Washing
ton last night which contained the infor
mation that Rear Admiral Kautz was or
dered to place the Boston out of com
mission and that repairs be made at once
Mare Island. The Mayor stated last
night that, Boston or no Boston, the pro
posed Illumination on Wednesday night
would take place, according to the pres
ent plans of the committee. Some of the
Boston boys might even be brought down
in a launch from Mare Island.

SUISUN GIVES GREETING
TO FATHER McKINNON
Reception Tendered the Chaplain of the
First California by His Former
Parishioners.
REV. FATHER W. D. McKINNON
SUISUN, Aug. 28.â reception ten-
dered to Father W. D. McKinnon,
chaplain of the First California
Regiment, just returned from the
Philippine Islands, was an event ;
long to be remembered by' the residents â¢
of Suisun and this part of Solano County. |
Although the time for preparation was i
short the ladies of the Red Cross Society, I
aided by the firemen and citizens, worked j
with an energy and zeal that produced
wonderful results, and the chaplain was j
accorded a welcome that was spontaneous j
and enthusiastic. All day long busy hands |
were at work making preparations for the !
reception. Decorations of red, white and
blue flags, streamers, vari-colored lan
terns, etc., were placed in positions, many !
of the business houses being decorated in i
an' elaborate manner.
A handsome arch was ' erected in the j
plaza, which was a mass of star-spangled
banners, surmounted by a well-executed I
portrait of Father McKinnon and lighted j
by a large number of electric lights, pre- |
senting a brilliant spectacle. When the j
evening train arrived a vast throng -was
assembled at the depot to welcome the re- â 
turning chaplain, and when he alighted
from the train he was received with huz
zas and cheers of welcome. A procession
was formed, consisting â¢ of some sixty
mounted horsemen, a brass band, car
riages filled with members of the Red
Cross Society, town officers, members of
the press and a long line of firemen and
citizens on foot. . :-v V'_ ::

Special Dispatch to The Call.
- PITTSBURG, Aug. -A splendid wel
come was glventhe brave Tenth Pennsyl
vania Volunteers to-day after more than
a year's gallant service in the Philippines.
The only thing lacking to make the day
one of supreme happiness was the ab
sence of the brave and well-beloved
Colonel Hawkins, whose death occurred
on the homeward bound transport. The
crowds that lined, the streets along the
route of the parade, from the parks in
Allegheny to the reviewing stand in
Schenely Park, was almost beyond count
ing. Good judges estimated the number
at not less than 500,000. Besides the fact
that, the soldiers were to be in the parade,
which in itself was a memorable sight, it
was also known that President McKinley,
Mrs. McKinley, Major General Merritt,
Major General Francis V. Greene and
other prominent national and State of
ficers were in the city.
The 'reception of the Philippine heroes
began early in .the morning at New
Brighton,- Pa., where a committee of
prominent citizens welcomed them as soon
as they had crossed the borders of the
State/ After: brief exercises and a hearty
breakfast, tendered by the citizens of
New Brighton, the regiment was rushed
Into Allegheny, .where the parade was
held.
Upon reaching Schenely Park the col
umn was reviewed by President McKinley,
after which he- proceeded tee the music
pavilion, where the exercises were held.
After music by the Economy band, Major
Lee Smith called the meeting to order and
Rev. L. N. Boyle, D. D.. delivered the in
vocation. Governor William A. Stone was
then introduced as chairman of the meet
ing and delivered an address of welcome
in behalf of the State.
President McKinley, being introduced,
said: '
Governor "Stone and Fellow-Citizens: I am
glad to participate with the families, friends
and fellow-citizens of the Tenth Pennsylvania
Volunteers in this glad reunion. You made
secure, and . permanent the victory of Dewey.
You added new glory to American arms. You
and your brave comrades engaged on other
fields of conflict have enlarged the map of the
United States and extended the Jurisdiction of
American liberty.
But while we share in the Joy that is ours.
there remain with us softened and hallowed
memories of those who went forth with you.
After marching through the principal
streets and Illuminating the heavens with
fireworks a halt was made at Native Sons'
Hall. This was quickly filled and the
ovation there tendered Father McKinnon
was full of that patriotism characteristic
of California. An impromptu programme
of music, consisting of national airs, was
rendered, after which an address of wel
come was made by Judge A. J. Buckles,
a veteran of the Civil War. who voiced
the sentiments of tlgse present in his
hearty words of welcome, his remarks be
ing frequently interrupted by the cheers
of the audience.
Father McKinnon then made an address
in reply, giving expression of the cam
paign in the Philippines and eulogizing in
the warmest terms the bravery and gal
lantry of the First California, relating
many incidents of the' heroism displayed
while under fire and while on duty at
Manila. In concluding his remarks he
deprecated the attacks that had been
made upon General Otis and his conduct
of the campaign, and asserted emphatical
ly that history would vindicate every act
of the general. He also gavq it as his
opinion that the warfare in the islands
would soon end, although a guerrilla war
fare might be continued for years.
At the conclusion, the audience arose
and sang "America," and then followed
a general Handshaking and greeting of the
gallant chaplain of the First Californians.
A banquet at the Union Hotel concluded
the reception, which was attended by
about thirty of the leading citizens of the
town and the festivities kept up until a
late hour. ,-'x:X'

Market Street
I not found in your ranks to-day. Your noble
| colonel, devoted to his men, beloved by his
command and respected by his superior
I officers, gave his life to his country with many
i others of his comrades. The nation sorrows
with the bereaved. The heroes died for their
country, and there is no nobler death.
Our troops represented the truth and con-
science, the pure and patriotism of their
country. They voluntarily remained in the
service and declared their purpose to stay
until their places could be filled by new levies.
and longer if the Government needed their
services, and they understood it was not to
I be in camp or garrison, free from danger, but
j on the battle line where exposure an.l death
1 confronted them and where both have ex
; acted their victims.
! The mighty army of volunteers and regulars
i numbering over 250.000 which last year re
! sponded to the call of the Government with
j alacrity without precedent or parallel, by the
j terms of their enlistment were to be mus-
t ered out. with all of the* regulars above
27.000 men, when peace with Spain was ef
j fected. Peace brought us the Philippines by
I treaty cession from Spain. Every step taken
was in obedience to the requirements of the
i legislation. It became our territory and Is
ours as much as the Louisiana purchase or
I Texas or Alaska. A body of insurgents in ho
I sense representing the sentiment of the people
of the islands disputed our lawful authority,
I and even before the ratification of the treaty
i by the American Senate were attacking the
very forces who fought for and secured 'their
freedom. The world will never knew the re-
straint of our soldiersâ their self-control under
1 the most exasperating conditions. For weeks
i subjected to the insult and duplicity of the in-
surgent leaders they preserved the status quo.
I remembering that they were under an order
: from their Government to sacredly observe the
terms of the protocol in letter and spirit and
j avoid all conflict except in defense pending the
negotiations of the treaty of peace. They were
not the aggressors. They did not begin hos
t tilities against the insurgents pending the
ratification of the treaty of peace in the
Senate, great 'as was their justification, be-
cause their orders from Washington forbade
it. I take all the responsibility for that direc
tion. General Otis only executed the orders of
his Government, and the soldiers, under great
provocation to strike back, obeyed. Until tlÂ»e
treaty was ratified we had no authority be-
yond Manila city, bay and harbor. Spain had
sued for peace. The truce and treaty were not
concluded. The .first blow was struck by the
insurgents. They assailed our sovereignty and
there will he no useless parleyâ no pause until
the Insurrection is suppressed an.l the islands
placed under the undisputed sovereignty of the
United States, a government which will do
Justice to all and at once encourage the best
efforts and aspirations of these distant people
and the highest development of the rich and
fertile lands.
Congressman John Dalzell followed in
an eloquent address, and on behalf of the
citizens of Western Pennsylvania present-
ed each of the commissioned officers a
sword, the chaplain a loving cup and
every man in the regiment a beautiful
I medal. __
WORKMEN UNEARTH
SOME SPANISH RELICS
FIND BONES, SPUR AND SWORD
ON SAN MATEO ROAD.
They Are Supposed to Have Belonged
to a Cavalier Who Long Ago
Met an Untimely Death.
The workmen who are grading for the
San Francisco and San Mateo road
yesterday unearthed a skull, several hu
man bones and some articles supposed to
be Spanish relics. The 'discovery was
made while digging out a trench just
south of Ocean View road and about three
hundred feet north of the county line.
The skull and most of the bones crum
bled to pieces when they were handled,
which shows that they must have been
buried for hundreds of years. The relics
consisted of a spur and three feet of rust
eaten metal shaped like a sword. The
sword also broke into fragments when
extricated from the soil which adhered
to it. :*;â¢â  â -â¢<â 'â¢â .*
The spur bore traces of exquisite Span
ish workmanship when the rust and red
dirt were scraped off. It is a cruel look
ing instrument, having six prongs, each
almost two Inches in length.
Whose the remains are and how long
they have been buried is but mere con
jecture, but it is most likely that some
cavalier in the suite of some Spanish dis
coverers of this coast met with an un
timely death. As there was no sign of
wood or a coffin around the remains it is
supposed that he died with his boots on
in some quarrel with his enemies or com-
E anions. After being shot he was hastily
burled where he fell. This conclusion is
arrived at from a number of small brass
tacks found near by. These tacks were
such as were used to nail leather in shoes.
The relics were about two feet under
ground and were thickly Incrusted with
dirt. The Glen Park people have con
tracted for their exhibition.

Death of Ex-Senator M. J. Donovan.
Ex-Senator M. J. Donovan died at his
ranch at Hanford Sunday, August 27. Mr.
Donovan suffered for a period of two
years with stomach troubles. His life
has been a very active and useful one-
He has filled the position of School Direc
tor and City Treasurer and represented
the city and county of San Francisco in
the State Senate, serving with Hon.
Frank McCoppin and other distinguished
men with honor to himself and his con
stituents. He carried on a painting busi
ness at 1171 Market street and has. paint
ed many of our largest private and pun
lie buildings in the city and State. He
was well and favorably known among
the business men and building trades ot
the city and State as an honest and up
right man in all his. dealings. No one
that ever applied to him for aid was re
fused. He leaves a widow and several
children to mourn his loss. He will ne
burled at Hanford on Tuesday. August ii.

SAN MATEO CARS WILL
BE PAINTED GREEN
WORK ON NEW ROADBED NEAR-
ING COMPLETION.
In Two Months Cars Will Be Operated
From Market Street to Holy Cross â¢
Cemetery Without Change.
The construction on the new roadway
of the San Francisco and San Mateo
Railway is going on rapidly and Superin
tendent Southard states that it will be
completed in about two manths. The
double track has been laid to Ocean View
and the ditch for one track has been
opened beyond Daly's Hill in San Mateo
county. All the joints of the rails are be
ing cast welded and a 20-ton roller is run
over the road, thus assuring a perfect
roadbed.
The thirty new cars are nearly all
built and the company has adopted a dark
olive green as the distinguishing color.
The old cars will be painted with the new
color as fast as practicable. Seventy cars
in all will be operated and they will run
from the ferry to the end of the road at
Holy Cross without change, a convenience
which will be appreciated. Other im
provements will be inaugurated and it is
intended to perfect the system in every
detail.

Peniel Missionary Convention.
A convention of the several Peniel mis
sions in this city and vicinity and
their friends will be begun this morning
at 11 o'clock and continue four days, with
meetings at 11 a.m. and 2:30 and 7:30 p. m.
The Rev. William E. Blackstone will
speak on Wednesday eveningâ subject,
"God's Committee, or Our Responsibility
to Evangelize the World." Mr. and Mrs.
Ferguson and other Peniel missionaries
will be in attendance and address the
meetings. A cordial invitation is extended
to all interested to attend.

Michael P. Jones' Will.
The will of Michael P. Jones, who died
on the 22d inst., leaving an estate valued
at over $500,000, was filed for probate yes
terday. The entire estate is devised to
Flora D. Jones, widow of the deceased.
